Divine Decree
intermediate
5 min
The Absurd Implication of Limiting Divine Power
Sanusi presents and refutes the objection by revealing its absurd implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that God can create acts by stripping human power leads to an absurd conclusion that undermines divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
Divine power cannot be overcome by any created power, even if the created power exists.

The debate over human free will and divine power often leads to flawed reasoning. Some argue that God can create an act by first removing the servant's power and will. But as Sanusi shows, this actually implies that when the servant has power, God is unable to act—a conclusion that no rational person can accept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part where Sanusi presents the opponents' claim. Note how they argue God can strip power and compel the act.
2
Find the absurd implication
Notice that Sanusi deduces that if God can only create when power is absent, then when power exists, God cannot act.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the simile
Read the simile of the master and slave. How does it illustrate the absurdity? What does the master have to do to overcome the slave?
The master must first remove the slave's power, analogously God must remove human power to act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك ولا ينفعهم ما يجيبون به من عدم لزوم عجزه تعالى عن الفعل الذي أوجده عبده ; قالوا : لأن تعالى قادر أن يوجد ذلك الفعل ; بأن يسلب عبده القدرة عليه والإرادة له ، ويلجئه إلى الفعل كما يفعل بالمرتعش ونحوه ; لأن نقول : عجز الإله وكونه مغلوباً على إيجاد ممكن ما .. يستحيل مطلقاً ، وهذا الجواب منهم اقتضى أن تعالى لا يتمكن من إيجاد فعل العبد إلا عند عدم قدرة العبد وإرادته ، أما مع وجودهما فإن ذلك الفعل الممكن يتعاصى عليه ، ولا يتمكن من إيجاده ، وتغلبه عليه قدرة العبد وإرادته ! فما أشبه ضلالهم هذا بمن يصف إنساناً بقوة عظيمة لا يغلبه معها أحد ، ولذلك الإنسان عبيد ، ويقول : إن ذلك السيد القوي في غاية لا يغلب واحداً من أولئك العبيد إلا إذا احتال عليه ; بأن يسلب أسباب القوة من الأكل ونحوه حتى لا تكون للعبد قدرة أصلاً ، أما إذا مكنه من الاتصاف بقدرة ، وإن كانت أضعف بكثير من قدرته . . لم يقدر أن يفعل معه فعلاً تتوجه إليه قدرة ذلك العبد وإرادته ، وصارت قدرة ذلك العبد وإرادته تغلبان قدرة سيده الموصوف بغاية القوة ! هذا نظير ما تخيلوه من الجواب ، فنعوذ بالله من الخذلان ، وأن تلعب بعقولنا التوهمات والشيطان على أن جوابهم المذكور لا يستقيم على أصلهم الفاسد من وجوب مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح عليه تعالى ، وأنه يستحيل في حقه تعالى أن يسلب العبد القدرة التي خلق له بعد أن كلفه ، بل يجب أن يمدّه بما تتيسر به عليه الأفعال
That, and their answer does not benefit them, which is the non-necessity of His, exalted, being incapable of the act which His servant brought into existence; they said: because the Exalted is able to bring that act into existence, by stripping His servant of power over it and will for it, and compelling him to the act just as He does with the tremulous and the like; because we say: the incapacity of the deity and His being overcome in bringing into existence some possible thing is absolutely impossible. And this answer of theirs implies that the Exalted is not able to bring about the act of the servant except when the servant lacks power and will; but as for when both exist, that possible act becomes stubborn towards Him, and He is not able to bring it into existence, and the servant's power and will overcome Him! How similar is their misguidance to one who describes a human being with tremendous strength that none can overcome, and that human being has slaves, and says: 'That strong master is at the utmost degree, he cannot overcome any of those slaves unless he schemes against him, by stripping him of the means of strength like food and such, so that the slave has no power at all; but if he enables him to be described with power, even if it is much weaker than his power, he cannot do an act with him to which the power and will of that slave are directed, and the power and will of that slave overcome the power of his master who is described with extreme strength!' This is analogous to what they imagined as an answer. So we seek refuge in Allah from abandonment, and that delusions and Satan play with our minds. Moreover, that mentioned answer of theirs is not sound upon their corrupt principle of the obligatory observance of what is beneficial and most beneficial for the Exalted, and that it is impossible in His right, exalted, to strip the servant of the power which He created for him after having obligated him; rather, He must supply him with what makes the acts easy for him.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised by an opposing theological school (likely the Mu'tazila or Qadariyya) regarding God's ability to create acts that the servant also performs. The author, Sanusi, presents their objection and then demolishes it through logical and analogical reasoning. First, he restates their claim: God can create the act by stripping the servant of power and will, compelling him like a trembling person. Sanusi counters that God being incapable of creating a possible act is absolutely impossible. The opponents' answer implies that God can only create the act when the servant has no power/will; otherwise, the act escapes God and human power prevails—an absurd implication. He then draws a striking simile: a master who is described as extremely strong but cannot overcome a weaker slave unless he first strips him of strength; if the slave retains any power, even much weaker, the master cannot act against him. This reveals the inconsistency of the opponents' position. Sanusi then adds a second critique: the opponents' answer contradicts their own principle that God must observe what is beneficial for His servants, since stripping power after obligation would be unjust according to their own premise. The passage proceeds in the author's order: first stating the objection, then refuting it with a reductio ad absurdum and a simile, then pointing out the internal inconsistency. The key point is that divine power cannot be limited by created power under any circumstance.

قدرة
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power is only effective when human power is absent
Correction: God's power is absolute and not conditional on the absence of human power. The opponents' answer implies the opposite, but this is absurd.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi claim the opponents' answer implies about God's ability when the servant has power?
A) God can still create the act
B) God cannot create the act
C) God uses the servant's power
D) God's power is unlimited

Key Takeaway
The opponents' objection that God can create acts by removing human power leads to the absurd conclusion that human power can overcome divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment today when you felt your own desire or plan was so strong that it seemed to override what Allah wants for you. How does Sanusi's argument expose that feeling as a delusion? What can you do right now to realign your heart with the truth that nothing can overcome Allah's power?
?
In what area of your life do you act as if your will is 'stubborn' against Allah's decree—perhaps in a habit you cling to or a relationship you control? If you truly believe no power can overcome His, what practical step will you take this week to submit that area to Him?
?
When you fear people's disapproval more than Allah's, you are implying that their power can overcome His promise to protect you. After reading this lesson, what specific situation comes to mind, and what will you change in your response to it starting today?

The Internal Contradiction in the Opponents' Principle
Sanusi argues that the opponents' answer contradicts their own principle of obligatory observance of what is beneficial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the opponents by showing that their answer contradicts their own doctrine of God's obligation to create what is beneficial.

Core Idea
Even within their own framework, the opponents' answer fails because it violates their principle of al-ṣalāḥ wa al-aṣlaḥ.

The previous lesson exposed the absurd implication of the opponents' answer. Now Sanusi delivers a second blow: the answer does not even cohere with the opponents' own theological principle that God must always do what is best for His servants. This internal inconsistency further weakens their position.

Guided Path
1
Recall the opponents' principle
The opponents believe God must consider what is beneficial. Review that concept.
2
See the contradiction
Sanusi argues that stripping power after obligation violates that principle. Why would it be impossible for God to strip power?
Because He already gave power to enable obedience.
3
Conclude the inconsistency
The opponents' answer fails even on their own terms.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised by an opposing theological school (likely the Mu'tazila or Qadariyya) regarding God's ability to create acts that the servant also performs. According to the principle of al-ṣalāḥ wa al-aṣlaḥ, what must God do after obligating the servant?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hold yourself to the same standard of consistency that you demand from others? When you discover a contradiction in your own beliefs or actions, do you adjust them to align with truth, or do you rationalize the inconsistency? This lesson exposes the danger of holding a principle while violating it. Ask yourself: Where in your life have you applied a double standard between what you claim to believe and how you actually live? Will you commit today to identifying one such inconsistency and correcting it, even if it costs you comfort or pride?
?
The opponents believed God must always do what is most beneficial. Yet they claimed He could strip power after obligating—a clear contradiction. Consider your own understanding of God's attributes. Do you ever limit God's mercy or justice by your own assumptions? Have you ever thought, 'God would not forgive me for that' or 'God is too harsh to help me in this situation'—even though His mercy is vast? This lesson reminds you that God's actions are consistent with His perfect attributes. How can you realign your expectations of God with what the Qur'an and Sunnah actually teach about His benevolence and justice?
?
Sanusi refutes his opponents using their own framework, not by imposing external standards. In your conversations with those who disagree with you about faith, do you seek to understand their premises and address them from within, or do you only repeat your own arguments without engaging their worldview? This lesson challenges you to learn the art of internal critique. The next time you discuss a theological or moral issue with someone who holds a different view, will you take the time to genuinely understand their principles and then show how those principles lead to the truth—just as Sanusi did? Commit to practicing this method in your next meaningful conversation.

Refutation of the Face Argument for Human Power
Sanusi refutes the face argument for human power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that the 'face' (wajh) of an action cannot be attributed to human power without leading to contradiction or compulsion.

Core Idea
The 'face' of an action either belongs to God or is nothing at all.

Sanusi addresses a subtle argument from certain theologians who claimed that actions have a specific 'face' that can be attributed to human power. He presents a devastating dilemma: either this face is possible or impossible. If possible, it must come from God; if impossible, it cannot come from anyone. The argument also exposes that their position leads to the very compulsion they tried to avoid. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the clear dilemma structure.
The passage is dense with conditional reasoning.
2
Understand the dilemma
Identify the two horns: if the 'face' is possible, it must be from God; if impossible, it cannot be from any power. See how this forces the opponents into a corner.
3
See the compulsion implication
Sanusi argues that the opponents' own view leads to compulsion (jabr) because the servant cannot intend the state as it is, and once God creates the essence, the servant cannot avoid it.
This shows their flight from compulsion actually necessitates it.
4
Special refutation of al-Ustadh
The final point is specifically against al-Ustadh: if the face is only in the intellect, how can intention be directed to something with no external existence?
This highlights a contradiction in their position.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this refutation strengthens the Ash'ari doctrine of divine omnipotence and refutes any attempt to assign power to the servant independently.
This is a classic tahlili argument in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيما أضافوه للعبدِ ؛ فإنَّ هذا الوجه إمَّا أنْ يكونَ ممكناً أو لا ، فإنْ كان ممكناً وجب إضافته إلى قدرةِ اللهِ تعالى ، وإن لم يكن ممكناً امتنع نسبته إلى قدرة ما. وما فرُّوا عنهُ مِنَ الجبرِ لازم لهم (۱) ؛ لأنَّ تلك الحالَ لا يُتصوَّرُ القصد إلى إيجادها على حيالها ، فلا يتأتّى مِنَ العبد فعلها ما لم يفعل الله تعالى تلكَ الذاتَ ، ومتى فعلَ الذات فلا يُتصوَّرُ مِنَ العبد تركها على زعمهم ، فكانَ الجبر لازماً لهم. وهذا على الأستاذ أشدُّ إلزاماً ؛ فإنَّ الوجه والاعتبار يكونُ في العقل ، فكيف يصح توجه القصدِ إلى فعل ما ليس له وجود في الخارج (۲) ؟! ) انتهى
Regarding what they add to the servant: this 'face' is either possible or not. If it is possible, its attribution to the power of God Most High is necessary; and if it is not possible, its attribution to any power is impossible. And what they flee from of compulsion is necessary for them (1); because that state cannot be intended to be brought about as it is, so the servant cannot perform it unless God Most High creates that essence, and when He creates the essence, the servant cannot abandon it according to their claim, so compulsion is necessary for them. And this is even more binding on al-Ustadh; for the face and consideration exist in the intellect, so how can the intention be directed to an act that has no existence in the external (2)?! End.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage refutes an argument by al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) and al-Ustadh (al-Isfara'ini) who claimed that actions have a specific 'face' (wajh) or consideration that is attributable to the servant's power. The author presents a dilemma: The 'face' they add is either possible or impossible. If possible, it must be attributed to God's power because all possible things are created by God. If impossible, it cannot be attributed to any power at all. Thus their position is untenable. Furthermore, the author argues that their flight from compulsion actually forces compulsion upon them: the supposed state cannot be intended as it is, so the servant's action depends entirely on God creating that essence, and once God creates it, the servant cannot avoid it, making compulsion necessary. The final point notes that this refutation is even stronger against al-Ustadh, who places the 'face' in the intellect: how can one intend an act that has no external existence? This highlights a contradiction in their view.

Key Terms
الوجه (al-wajh)
الجبر (al-jabr)
الأستاذ (al-Ustadh)
القدرة (al-qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That actions have a 'face' that can be attributed to human power independently of God.
Correction: Sanusi shows that such a face either must be from God (if possible) or is nothing (if impossible), making independent human attribution impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what is the problem with the 'face' (al-wajh) that the opponents add to the servant?
A) It is always impossible, so it cannot be attributed to any power.
B) It is either possible or impossible; if possible it must be from God, if impossible it cannot be from any power.
C) It is always possible, so it must be attributed to the servant.
D) It is the same as the essence of the act and therefore irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The 'face' of an action cannot be attributed to human power without contradiction, because it either belongs to God or is non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you ever catch yourself feeling that the act is 'yours'—something you accomplished independently? How does Sanusi's insistence that every possible 'face' belongs solely to God challenge that feeling, and what would it actually mean to live as if every act of yours is entirely God's creation?
?
Think of a moment when you failed to act as you should, and you blamed your own weakness or lack of willpower. Did you ever consider that even that failure is created by God, and that your 'will' itself is not an independent power? How does the argument that the face argument inevitably leads to compulsion make you reconsider the very nature of your intentions?
?
If the 'face' of an action is either God's or nothing, then your intention to act can only be directed toward something God has already brought into existence. How does this reshape your planning for tomorrow? Will you still plan, pray, and strive, knowing that the outcome—and even your desire—is entirely from Him? What changes in your heart when you truly internalize that?

Five Opinions on Created Capacity
Sanusi enumerates five opinions on created capacity and declares only the Ash'ari view correct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five theological opinions on created capacity and why only the Ash'ari view is considered correct.

Core Idea
Created capacity has no causal effect; it merely accompanies the act.

This passage from Sanusi's creedal work presents a concise survey of Islamic theological positions on the nature of human capacity (qudra). The correct view, according to Sanusi, is that of Imam al-Ash'ari: human capacity has no effect whatsoever on bringing about actions; it is merely concurrent with the act. The other four opinions are systematically listed and rejected. As we read, notice how Sanusi anchors his position in scripture and consensus, while expressing surprise at learned scholars who reportedly held contrary views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five opinions
Read the passage and list the five opinions in order. Focus on what each one says about the role of created capacity.
The first opinion is the Ash'ari view, which is defended.
2
Examine the Ash'ari view
Note the key phrase: 'no effect at all' (la ta'thir laha albatta). Understand that capacity is merely concomitant with the act, not its cause.
This position is said to be from the Book, Sunnah, and consensus of the salaf.
3
Analyze the false opinions
Understand why each of the other four opinions is rejected. Compare the roles they assign to created capacity.
Notice the sectarian labels: Jabriyya, Qadariyya.
4
Reflect on Sanusi's astonishment
Consider why Sanusi is amazed at the views attributed to Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi al-Baqillani, given their own writings.
This highlights the importance of consistent doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحاصل في القدرة الحادثة خمسة أقوال ] قلتُ : والحاصلُ : أَنَّ الأقوال في هذه المسألة خمسة : قولُ الأشعري ومَنْ تابعَهُ - وهو الذي دل عليه الكتاب والسنة ، وأجمع عليه سلف الأمة - : أنَّ قدرة العبدِ لا تأثير لها ألبتةَ ، وإنَّما هي مقارنة لمقدورها فقط. والثاني : القولُ الذي حُكِي عن الإمام (۱) : أنَّ القدرة الحادثة تُؤثر في وجود الفعل على أقدار قدَّرَها البارى تعالى. والثالث : قول القاضى ومَنْ تابعَهُ : أَنَّها تؤثر في أخص وصفِ الفعل ، لا في وجودِهِ . والرابع : مذهبُ الجبريَّةِ : أَنَّهُ لا قدرة أصلاً ، وإنَّما المخلوقُ للعبدِ المقدورُ فقط ؛ كالحركة والسكون مثلاً ، ولا زيادة عليه أصلاً ، وساوَوا بين المضطر كالمرتعش وبين المختار. والخامس : مذهبُ القدريَّةِ مجوس هذه الأمة : أنَّ القدرة الحادثة تُؤثر في وجودِ الفعل على سبيل الاستقلال . وهذه الأقوال كلُّها باطلةٌ ما عدا الأوَّلَ ، وإِيَّاهُ اعتمدت في هذهِ العقيدة ، وهو الحقُّ الذي لا شكّ فيه ، وأنا أعجبُ مِنَ القولِ الذي نُقِلَ عن الإمام كيف يصحُ أنْ يقولهُ معَ ما أكثرَ في ( الإرشادِ » وغيرِهِ مِنَ الأدلَّةِ لتصحيح المذهب الحقِّ ؛ وهو مذهب الأشعري ، ومبالغتِهِ في النكير والتضليل لمَنْ يعتقد أنَّ للقدرة الحادثة تأثيراً ما ، وكذا ما نُقِلَ عن القاضي والأستاذ مع ما لهما في تواليفهما ممَّا يُضادُّهُ !
The sum regarding created capacity is five opinions. I say: The sum is that the opinions on this issue are five: First: The opinion of al-Ash'ari and those who followed him - which is what the Book and the Sunnah indicate, and the predecessors of the community agreed upon - that the servant's capacity has absolutely no effect, and it is merely concomitant with its object of power only. Second: The opinion reported from al-Imam (1) that the created capacity influences the existence of the action according to measures determined by the Creator Most High. Third: The opinion of al-Qadi and those who followed him that it influences the most specific description of the act, not its existence. Fourth: The doctrine of the Jabriyya that there is no capacity at all, and only the determined object is created for the servant, like motion and stillness, etc., with no addition at all; they equated the compelled person like the one with a tremor and the choiceful person. Fifth: The doctrine of the Qadariyya, the Magians of this community, that the created capacity influences the existence of the act independently. All these opinions are false except the first, and it is what I have relied upon in this creed, and it is the truth about which there is no doubt. I am amazed at the opinion reported from al-Imam as to how he could say it given his many proofs in al-Irshad and other works to correct the true doctrine, which is the doctrine of al-Ash'ari, and his extreme criticism and misguidance of those who believe the created capacity has any effect; likewise what was reported from al-Qadi and al-Ustadh given what they have in their works that contradicts it!
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage enumerates the five opinions regarding created capacity (al-qudra al-haditha) in human actions. The author first states the correct Ash'ari view: the servant's capacity has absolutely no effect in producing the action; it merely accompanies the action (mujarrad muqārana). Then he lists the other four views: (2) the view attributed to Imam al-Haramayn that created capacity influences the existence of the act according to measures predetermined by God; (3) the view of al-Qadi al-Baqillani that it influences only the most specific description (akhass wasf) of the act, not its existence; (4) the Jabri view that there is no capacity at all, likening the human to a trembling person; (5) the Qadari (Mu'tazili) view that created capacity independently produces the act. The author declares all but the first false, emphasizing that he follows the Ash'ari doctrine. He expresses astonishment that Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi could be reported to hold contrary views, given their own writings against such positions.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة (al-qudra al-haditha)
المقدور (al-maqdur)
أخص وصف (akhass wasf)
الجبرية (al-Jabriyya)
القدرية (al-Qadariyya)
الأشعري (al-Ash'ari)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Ash'ari view means human power is completely irrelevant and actions are forced upon humans.
Correction: The Ash'ari view affirms that humans have capacity and free choice, but this capacity does not causally produce the action; it accompanies it. The action is created by God alone.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which opinion on created capacity is correct?
A) The capacity influences the existence of the act according to preordained measures.
B) The capacity influences the most specific description of the act.
C) The capacity has no effect at all and merely accompanies the object of power.
D) The capacity independently produces the act.

Key Takeaway
The only correct position on created capacity is the Ash'ari view that it has no causal effect but merely accompanies the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something good, do you feel a sense of pride as if you caused it? Read the source again: the first opinion says capacity has absolutely no effect. How can you train your heart to attribute all success to Allah, especially in moments of personal achievement? Commit to a specific dua or practice to guard against hidden shirk in your deeds.
?
The Jabriyya equated the compelled with the choiceful. Think of a sin you have justified by saying 'I couldn't help it' or 'it was out of my control.' Does that excuse reflect a subtle fatalism? How does the Ash'ari position—that your capacity is present but not the cause—give you both responsibility for your actions and dependence on Allah? What one change will you make to own your choices without despairing?
?
Sanusi declares that all opinions except the first are false. Is your daily belief about your own ability in line with this? When you make a mistake, do you truly feel that your capacity was present but not the cause of the error? How would this awareness transform your repentance and your trust in Allah's mercy?

The Doctrine of Kasb as the Middle Way
Sanusi defines kasb, refutes jabr and qadariyyah, and warns against misunderstanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash‘arī doctrine of kasb as the middle way between fatalism and free will, and why it preserves divine oneness while grounding moral responsibility.

Core Idea
Kasb is the middle path: human action is acquired, not created, by the servant.

Sanusi now turns to one of the most delicate topics in kalām: how to affirm human responsibility without compromising divine power. He presents the Ash‘arī concept of kasb as the mean between two extremes — the Jabriyyah who deny human agency and the Qadariyyah who claim independent creation. This passage defines kasb, refutes both errors, and warns against a common misunderstanding that would collapse the middle position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation. Let Sanusi's argument unfold naturally.
2
Identify the definition of kasb
Find where Sanusi states what Ahl al-Sunnah mean by kasb. What is its relationship to human power?
Kasb is simultaneous without effect.
3
Note the two refuted sects
Sanusi refutes al-Jabriyyah and al-Qadariyyah. Identify the core error of each and the reason each is invalid.
Jabriyyah deny the originated power; Qadariyyah claim the servant creates his actions.
4
Understand the common misunderstanding
Many mistakenly think kasb implies human power has an effect. Sanusi rejects this. Why would that corrupt the doctrine?
Because effect would imply a partner in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الكلام في الكسب تعلق هذه القدرة الحادثة بالمقدور مقارنةً لهُ مِنْ غير تأثير .. عبَّرَ أهل السنَّةِ رضي الله عنهم بالكسب ؛ وهو متعلَّقُ التكليف الشرعى ، وأمارة على الثواب والعقاب فبطل إذاً مذهبُ الجبرِ ؛ وهو إنكار القدرة الحادثة ؛ لما فيهِ مِنْ جَحْدِ الضرورةِ ، وإبطالِ محلّ التكليف ، وأمارة الثواب والعقاب ، ومِنْ هنا كانَ بدعة ، ومذهب القدريَّةِ ؛ وهو كونُ العبدِ يخترع أفعاله على وَفْقِ مرادِهِ بالقدرة التي خلق الله تعالى لهُ ؛ لما علمتَ مِنْ دليل الوحدانيَّةِ ، واستحالة شريك معَ اللهِ تعالى أيّاً كانَ . هذا تفسير للكسب الذي قالَ بهِ أهلُ السنَّةِ رضيَ اللهُ تعالى عنهم وهو درجة وسطى بين مذهبي الجبريَّةِ والقدريَّةِ ، وكثيراً ما يتوهَّمُ مَنْ معنى الكسب كونُ القدرة الحادثة لها تأثير ما ! وهذا لا علم عندَهُ أَنَّ التأثيرَ الذي يُفسِّرُ به الجاهل معنى الكسب : إنْ أرادَ : أنَّ القدرة الحادثةَ تُؤثر في حال الفعل كما يُحكى عن القاضي والأستاذ . . فقد تقدَّمَ فساد هذا القول ، وعدم جريانه على
And regarding the discussion on kasb: The relation of this originated power (al-qudrah al-hādithah) to the capable thing (al-maqdūr) is simultaneous with it without effect. Ahl al-Sunnah, may Allah be pleased with them, expressed it as kasb (acquisition); and it is the locus of shar‘ī obligation and an indication of reward and punishment. Thus the doctrine of al-jabr (fatalism) is nullified; it is the denial of the originated power, because it involves denying necessity (al-ḍarūrah), nullifying the locus of obligation, and the indication of reward and punishment. Hence it is a bid‘ah (innovation). And the doctrine of al-qadariyyah (free will) is that the servant innovates (yakhtari‘u) his actions in accordance with his own will by the power which Allah the Exalted created for him; because you know from the proof of tawḥīd (divine oneness) and the impossibility of any partner with Allah the Exalted. This is the interpretation of kasb which Ahl al-Sunnah, may Allah the Exalted be pleased with them, held, and it is a middle degree between the two doctrines of al-Jabriyyah and al-Qadariyyah. And often one misunderstands the meaning of kasb to be that the originated power has some effect! And this person has no knowledge that the effect which the ignorant person interprets as the meaning of kasb: if he meant that the originated power has an effect in the state of the act as is narrated from al-Qāḍī and al-Ustādh... then the corruption of this statement has already preceded, and its non-applicability upon...
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage defines and defends the Ash‘arī doctrine of kasb (acquisition). The author begins by explaining that the relationship between the human’s originated power (qudrah hādithah) and the action (maqdūr) is one of concomitance without causal effect. Ahl al-Sunnah call this relationship 'kasb'. This kasb is what makes an action the subject of shar‘ī obligation and the indicator for reward and punishment. Then the author refutes two extreme positions: (1) Al-Jabriyyah, who deny the existence of any human power at all, which would nullify the very basis of obligation and moral responsibility; thus it is a bid‘ah. (2) Al-Qadariyyah, who claim that the human creates his own actions independently using the power Allah gave him; this is refuted by tawḥīd and the impossibility of any partner with Allah. Then the author reiterates that kasb is the middle path between these two extremes. Finally, he addresses a common misunderstanding: some people mistakenly think that kasb implies that the human power has some real effect (ta’thīr). The author argues that such an effect, if understood as the human power causing the action (as attributed to al-Qāḍī and al-Ustādh), has already been shown to be corrupt and cannot be maintained. The passage ends abruptly, cutting off the conclusion.

Key Terms
الكسب
القدرة الحادثة
التكليف
الختراع
الوحدانية
الجبرية
القدريّة
تأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think kasb means the human's originated power has a real effect on the action.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that any such effect would imply a partner in creation, which is impossible; kasb is merely concomitance without causal efficacy.

Checkpoint
What is the role of kasb (acquisition) according to Ahl al-Sunnah as explained in this passage?
A) It is the creative act of the human.
B) It is the locus of shar‘ī obligation and an indication of reward and punishment.
C) It is the denial of human power.
D) It is the effect of the human power on the action.

Key Takeaway
Kasb is the Ash‘arī middle way: it affirms human responsibility without attributing creative power to the servant, preserving divine oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading that jabr nullifies the very basis of obligation and judgment, when in your life have you used the idea that 'everything is written' to avoid taking responsibility for a sin? What would it look like to face that sin as an act you acquired and must answer for, without blaming God?
?
The source refutes qadariyyah with the proof that no partner can share in creation with Allah. In what areas of your daily routine do you subtly act as if you are the sole cause of your successes—your career, your knowledge, your relationships? How would acknowledging your complete dependence on Allah change your attitude before and after every action?
?
Kasb is described as a 'middle degree' between two extremes. Think about your own spiritual state: do you tend toward a fatalistic passivity (jabr) or an arrogant self-reliance (qadariyyah) when facing challenges or trials? Name one concrete situation this week where you will consciously adopt the attitude of kasb—taking full responsibility while knowing your power has no effect without Allah's creation.

The Definition of Kasb and Refutation of Two Extremes
Sanusi defines kasb and refutes Jabriyya and Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb and how it simultaneously refutes the Jabriyya and Qadariyya sects.

Core Idea
Kasb: created power accompanies the act without causing it.

The passage you are about to read is a classic statement of the Ash'ari position on human action. It defines a technical term—kasb (acquisition)—that resolves the tension between divine power and human responsibility. Then it uses that definition to dismiss two extreme schools: the Jabriyya, who deny human power entirely, and the Qadariyya, who claim humans create their own acts. Pay attention to how the definition itself does the work of refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once for its overall flow. Then read the translation. Notice how the definition of kasb is stated first, then applied to refute two sects.
The passage has a clear three-part structure: definition, refutation of Jabriyya, refutation of Qadariyya.
2
Understand the definition of kasb
Identify what kasb means: the created power (qudrah hādithah) attaches to the act (maqdūr) concurrently but without causing it. This is a 'middle path' between compulsion and independent creation.
The phrase 'min ghayr ta'thīr' (without effect) is crucial.
3
See why Jabriyya is refuted
Jabriyya denies the created power entirely. According to the passage, this leads to denying self-evident necessity (al-ḍarūrah) and nullifying the basis of religious obligation and signs of reward and punishment. Hence it is an innovation (bid'ah).
4
See why Qadariyya is refuted
Qadariyya claims the servant creates his own acts. The refutation relies on the proof of divine oneness (dalīl al-waḥdāniyyah): if the servant were a creator, there would be a partner with Allah, which is impossible.
This refutation appeals to tawḥīd, not just kasb theory.
5
Reflect on the middle path
Consider how the Ash'ari position avoids both extremes: it affirms a real created power that makes the servant responsible (for taklīf) but denies causal efficacy to preserve divine unity.
This is the broader theological significance of the passage.

Source Text

وعن الكلام في الكتب تعلق هذه القدرة الحادثة بالمقدور مقارنةً لهُ مِنْ غير تأثير .. عبَّرَ أهل السنَّةِ رضي الله عنهم بالكسب ؛ وهو متعلَّقُ التكليف الشرعى ، وأمارة على الثواب والعقاب فبطل إذاً مذهبُ الجبرِ ؛ وهو إنكار القدرة الحادثة ؛ لما فيهِ مِنْ جَحْدِ الضرورةِ ، وإبطالِ محلّ التكليف ، وأمارة الثواب والعقاب ، ومِنْ هنا كانَ بدعة ، ومذهب القدريَّةِ ؛ وهو كونُ العبدِ يخترع أفعاله على وَفْقِ مرادِهِ بالقدرة التي خلق الله تعالى لهُ ؛ لما علمتَ مِنْ دليل الوحدانيَّةِ ، واستحالة شريك معَ اللهِ تعالى أيّاً كانَ
And concerning speech in the books: the attachment of this created power (al-qudrah al-hādithah) to the act (al-maqdūr) accompanying it without effect – the People of the Sunnah (Ahl al-Sunnah), may Allah be pleased with them, expressed it as acquisition (al-kasb). And it is the object of religious obligation (al-taklīf al-sharʿī) and a sign of reward and punishment. So the school of al-Jabr (al-Jabriyyah) is nullified, which is the denial of the created power, because it involves rejection of necessity, nullification of the locus of obligation, and nullification of the sign of reward and punishment, and therefore it is an innovation. And the school of al-Qadariyyah is nullified, which is that the servant creates his actions according to his wish by the power that Allah Most High created for him, because you have known from the proof of oneness (al-waḥdāniyyah) and the impossibility of a partner with Allah Most High, whoever it may be.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'kasb' as used by the People of the Sunnah to describe the relationship between the created power and the act. The author explains that the created power attaches to the act concomitantly without any causal effect. Kasb is the locus of religious obligation and a sign for reward and punishment. Then the author refutes two deviant schools: the Jabriyya, who deny the created power entirely, thus rejecting self-evident necessity and invalidating the basis of obligation; and the Qadariyya, who claim the servant creates his own actions independently using the power Allah created for him, which contradicts the proof of divine oneness and the impossibility of a partner for Allah. The refutation is based on the established doctrines.

Correction: The passage explicitly says the created power attaches 'without effect' (min ghayr ta'thīr).

Checkpoint

D) The created power causes the act with Allah's permission.

Key Takeaway
Kasb is the middle path: the servant's power accompanies the act without causing it, preserving both human responsibility and divine oneness.

?

?
Consider a habit you struggle to change. Do you act as if you have no power (Jabriyya) or as if you can do it entirely on your own (Qadariyya)? What would it mean to embrace the middle path of kasb in that struggle?
?
If your created power is not the cause of your actions, then every good deed is a gift from Allah. How does this truth affect the way you thank Him and the humility you bring to your worship today?

Kasb as a Middle Way and Refutation of Three Misinterpretations
Sanusi refutes three false interpretations of kasb that attribute causal effect to created power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that kasb is a middle path between Jabriyya and Qadariyya and how Sanusi refutes three false interpretations that attribute any causal effect to the created power.

Core Idea
Kasb is a middle way: no independent creation by man, no pure coercion—only acquisition by divine grant.

This passage defends the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb (acquisition) as the balanced position on human action. Sanusi warns against common misunderstandings that ascribe causal power to the created capacity, and he systematically refutes three wrong interpretations attributed to earlier scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation to grasp the full argument.
Notice the repeated conditional structure 'If he intends...' which frames the three refutations.
2
Identify the definition of kasb as a middle way
The first sentence states that kasb is a middle degree between Jabriyya (coercion) and Qadariyya (independent creation). Commit this as the core meaning.
This definition is the anchor for the entire discussion.
3
Examine the three refuted interpretations
The passage lists three wrong understandings of kasb: (1) created power influences the state of the act, (2) it influences existence but not independently, (3) it is granted to the servant for independent action. Notice how each is rejected.
The refutations use earlier evidence and warn against following scholars' slips.
4
Understand the principle of not following slips
Sanusi emphasizes that even a great scholar's mistake in fundamentals is not to be followed. This reinforces the importance of correct doctrine.
This principle is a key takeaway for dealing with scholarly disagreements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا تفسير للكسب الذي قالَ بهِ أهلُ السنَّةِ رضيَ اللهُ تعالى عنهم وهو درجة وسطى بين مذهبي الجبريَّةِ والقدريَّةِ ، وكثيراً ما يتوهَّمُ مَنْ معنى الكسب كونُ القدرة الحادثة لها تأثير ما ! وهذا لا علم عندَهُ أَنَّ التأثيرُ الذي يُفسِّرُ به الجاهل معنى الكسب : إنْ أرادَ : أنَّ القدرة الحادثةَ تُؤثر في حال الفعل كما يُحكى عن القاضي والأستاذ . . فقد تقدَّمَ فساد هذا القول ، وعدم جريانه على السنة ، وتقدَّمَ إنكارُ الشريف شارحِ الأسرار العقليَّةِ » صدور هذا القولِ مِنَ القاضي والأستاذ(۱) وإنْ أرادَ : أَنَّها تؤثر في وجودِ المقدور ، لكن بمشيئة الله تعالى لا على الاستقلال كما يُحكى عن إمام الحرمين في آخرِ أمرِه .. فقد تقدَّمَ أيضاً فساد هذا القولِ (۲) ، وتشعُبُهُ مِنْ مذهب القدرية مجوس هذهِ الأمة ، والظنُّ بالإمامِ رضيَ اللهُ عنهُ أنَّه لا يرضى بمثل هذا القول وعلى تقدير أن يكون صدرَ منه - ولا حول ولا قوَّةَ إلا بالله - فزلَّةُ العالم لا يجوزُ أنْ يُقلَّدَ فيها في الفروع ، فكيف بالعقائدِ أصول الدين ؟! وإِنْ أرادَ : أَنَّ القدرة الحادثة خلقها الله تعالى للعبدِ ومَلَّكَ لَهُ أَنْ يفعل المقدور بها كيف شاء على سبيل الاستقلال . . فهذا عين مذهب القدرية مجوس هذهِ الأُمَّةِ
This is an explanation of the acquisition (al-kasb) which the People of the Sunnah, may Allah Most High be pleased with them, held, and it is a middle degree between the two schools of al-Jabriyyah and al-Qadariyyah. And many people often imagine that the meaning of kasb is that the created power has some effect! But he has no knowledge that the effect by which the ignorant one interprets the meaning of kasb: if he intends that the created power influences the state of the act, as is narrated from al-Qāḍī and al-Ustādh... then the corruption of this statement and its not being in accordance with the Sunnah has already preceded, and the rejection by al-Sharīf, the commentator of 'al-Asrār al-ʿAqliyyah', of the issuance of this statement from al-Qāḍī and al-Ustādh has preceded (1). And if he intends that it influences the existence of the act, but by the will of Allah Most High, not independently, as is narrated from Imām al-Ḥaramayn at the end of his life... then the corruption of this statement has also preceded (2), and its branching off from the school of al-Qadariyyah, the Magians of this nation. And the assumption about the Imām, may Allah be pleased with him, is that he would not be pleased with such a statement. And assuming it issued from him – and there is no power nor strength except by Allah – then the slip of a scholar is not to be followed (taqlīd) in subsidiary matters, so how about in beliefs, the fundamentals of religion?! And if he intends that the created power Allah Most High created for the servant and granted him to do the act with it however he wishes independently... then this is exactly the school of al-Qadariyyah, the Magians of this nation.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage further clarifies the concept of kasb as a middle way between Jabriyya (coercion) and Qadariyya (free creation). The author warns that many misunderstand kasb to imply an effect of the created power. He then refutes three specific misinterpretations: First, the view (attributed to al-Qāḍī and al-Ustādh) that the created power influences the state of the act – this is previously refuted as contrary to the Sunnah. Second, the view (attributed to Imām al-Ḥaramayn at the end of his life) that it influences the existence of the act but not independently, only with Allah's will – this too is refuted as branching from Qadariyya. The author comments that even if such a slip occurred from a great scholar, it cannot be followed in principles. Third, the view that the created power is given to the servant as an independent creator – this is the very school of Qadariyya. Thus, the only correct meaning is the one originally defined.

?

?

When you face trials or your plans go awry, do you ever feel resentment or doubt towards Allah's decree as if you deserved a different outcome? How can you cultivate the inward conviction that all that happens is from Allah, and that your only role is to submit and acquire with sincere intention?

The Exact Meaning of Kasb and Its Relation to Taklif
Sanusi clarifies the precise meaning of kasb and its role in taklif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of kasb according to Ahl al-Sunnah and why it is the basis for religious obligation.

Core Idea
Kasb is not causation; it is simply the simultaneous existence of act and human power.

Sanusi now clarifies exactly what Ahl al-Sunnah mean by kasb, a term often misunderstood. He shows that kasb refers only to the attachment of the created power to the act, without any effect. He then explains why this is the object of religious obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical clue for restriction
Notice that Sanusi placed the genitive phrase 'concerning the attachment' before its verb to indicate restriction (ḥaṣr). This tells us that the only meaning of kasb is this attachment, nothing else.
In Arabic, preposing a phrase often restricts meaning.
2
Understand what kasb is NOT
Sanusi explicitly denies that kasb means the created power has any effect on the action. This refutes the view of those who think Ahl al-Sunnah allow a causal role for human power.
The 'ignorant misguided ones' are those who misinterpret kasb as a type of causation.
3
Connect kasb to taklif
Sanusi states that this conjunction of act and power (i.e., kasb) is what the Law addresses when it obliges actions. An act occurring without the created power (e.g., forced movement) is not subject to obligation.
The text cuts off, but the implication is that taklif requires the presence of human power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنَّما مراد أهل السنَّةِ بالكسب : ما أشرتُ إليه في أصل العقيدة ؛ فقولي : ( عن تعلَّقِ ) يتعلَّقُ بـ ( عبَّرَ ) ، وإِنَّما قدَّمْتُ هذا المجرور على عامله لإفادة الحصرِ ؛ أي: لا معنى للكسب إلا هذا ، لا أنَّ معناه أنَّ للقدرةِ الحادثة تأثيراً ما كما تعتقده الجهلة الضالُّونَ في معنى الكسب الذي هو مذهب أهل السنة وقولي : ( وهو مُتعلَّقُ التكليف الشرعي ) أي : الكسب ـ وهو وجود المقدور معَ القدرةِ الحادثة - هو الذي كلَّفَ به الشرع فيما كلَّفَ ؛ لأنَّ وقوع ذلك المقدور عارياً عن القدرة الحادثة - كحركة به
And the only intent of the People of the Sunnah regarding acquisition (al-kasb) is what I indicated in the original creed. So my saying: 'concerning the attachment' (ʿan taʿalluqi) is attached to 'expressed' (ʿabbara), and I only placed this genitive phrase before its operator to indicate restriction (al-ḥaṣr); that is, there is no meaning for kasb except this, not that its meaning is that the created power has an effect, as the ignorant misguided ones believe regarding the meaning of kasb which is the school of Ahl al-Sunnah. And my saying: 'and it is the object of religious obligation (al-taklīf al-sharʿī)' means: acquisition (al-kasb) – which is the existence of the act (al-maqdūr) together with the created power – is what the Law has obligated in what it has obligated; because the occurrence of that act devoid of the created power – like a movement with it...
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion by restating the precise meaning of kasb. The author explains his earlier phrasing: he placed 'concerning the attachment' before 'expressed' to indicate restriction (ḥaṣr), meaning kasb refers only to the attachment of the created power to the act without effect. He emphatically denies that kasb implies any effect of the created power, contrary to the ignorant. He then explains that 'kasb is the object of religious obligation' means that the act occurring together with the created power is what the Law addresses, because an act occurring without the created power (like a forced movement) is not subject to obligation. The text cuts off, but the argument is about the necessity of the created power for obligation.

Key Takeaway
Kasb, in the Sunni creed, is the coinciding of the act with the created power, without any causal effect; and it is precisely this conjunction that makes an action subject to religious obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The misguided ones claim that human power has an effect. Sanusi labels them 'ignorant and misguided'. Have you ever felt that your choices alone caused your good deeds? Repent of any hidden sense of self-sufficiency and renew your trust that all power belongs to Allah.

6 min
Jabriyya Refuted: Obligation Requires Capacity
Explains why refutation of Jabriyya follows from earlier proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation of Jabriyya follows from the earlier proof of acquired capacity, and why Jabr necessarily destroys the basis for divine obligation.

Core Idea
Jabriyya's determinism erases the distinction between voluntary and involuntary acts, undermining divine obligation.

In the previous lesson, Sanusi established that humans have an acquired capacity and that it does not cause its effect. Now he draws the crucial consequence: the doctrine of Jabriyya (absolute determinism) is not only false but a heresy that nullifies the entire framework of religious obligation. The Law distinguishes between acts within a person’s normal capacity and those that are not, and only the former can be subjects of divine command. If all acts are equally determined by God, then no act is truly within the servant’s power, and obligation becomes impossible. This lesson explains that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
The passage is a teacher’s commentary, so expect explanatory language.
2
Identify the two proofs summarized
The passage begins by stating that the refutation of Jabr is a consequence of two earlier proofs: establishing the acquired capacity and nullifying its effect. Notice how these proofs are repeated here in summary.
3
Understand the necessary distinction
The phrase 'denial of the necessary' refers to the necessary distinction between involuntary movement and acquired action. Jabriyya denies this distinction, which even a basic intellect perceives.
This is the first of two reasons why Jabr is problematic.
4
Grasp the clash with revelation
The second and more serious reason is that Jabr clashes with the Law, which obligates only acts within the servant’s normal capacity. If all acts were equal (as Jabr claims), no act is within capacity, so obligation is impossible—nullifying the Quran, Sunna, and consensus.
This leads to the conclusion that Jabr is a heresy affecting faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فبطل إذاً مذهب الجبرِ ... ) إلى آخرِهِ : مُسبَّبٌ عمَّا سبقَ قبلُ مِنْ دليلي إثباتِ القدرةِ الحادثة وإبطال تأثيرها في مقدورها ، وقد أعاد الدليلين هنا على سبيل الإجمال
فقوله : ( لما فيهِ مِنْ جَحْدِ الضرورة ) أي : الضرورة التي تقدَّمَتْ في الفرق بين حركة الاضطرار والاكتساب .
وقوله : ( وإبطال ( مخفوض بالعطف على ( جحْدِ ) يعني : : أَنَّهُ لو لم يكن في مذهبِ الجبرِ إلا ثبوتُ جهل بأمرٍ يُدرَكُ ضرورةً مِنْ غيرِ مصادمة للشريعة .. لكانَ أمره سهلاً ؛ إذْ غاية ما يلزم فيه التناهي في الغباوةِ وضَعْفِ العقلِ ، كيف والمذهب مصادم للشريعة ؟! لأنها قد جاءَتْ بإسقاط التكليف بالأفعال التي لا يتمكَّنُ العبد فيها عادةً مِنَ الاتصاف بوجودها وعدمها ، وبالتكليف بما يتيسر منها على العبدِ عادةً فعله وتركُهُ ، ولا تأثيرَ لهُ في شيءٍ مِنْ أفعالِهِ حتى يصح لنا التفريقُ كما تزعم القدريَّةُ ، فلم يبقَ ما يُفرَّقُ بهِ بينَ مَا يُكَلِّفُ بِهِ الشرعُ وما لا يكلِّفُ بهِ .. إلا الاكتساب على المعنى الذي سبق في تفسيره وعدمه . ولو استوَتِ الأفعالُ كلُّها كما يقولُ أهل الجبر .. لبطل تفريق الشرع بينها ، وبطل ما أحالَ عليهِ التكليف منها ؛ وهو الفعلُ الذي في وُسْعَ المكلَّف دونَ غيرِهِ ، وكانَتِ الأفعالُ حينَئِذٍ لا شيء منها في وُسْعِ المكلَّف عادةً ، فلا تكليف إذاً بشيء منها ؛ لقولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ لَا يُكَلِّفُ اللَّهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٨٦] ، وهذا إبطال للكتاب والسنَّةِ وإجماع الأمة ، وإلى هذا أشرتُ بقولي : ( ومن هنا كانَ بدعةً ) أي : ومِنْ أجلِ لزوم إبطال الجبر لمحلَّ التكليف الشرعي ولزوم انتفاء أمارة الثواب والعقاب . . كانَ بدعةً مُؤثرةً في عقدِ الإِيمَانِ
His statement: 'Thus the doctrine of Jabr is refuted...' to its end: is a consequence of what preceded earlier from the two proofs of establishing the acquired capacity and nullifying its effect in its object of power, and he has repeated the two proofs here in summary. So his statement: 'because it involves denial of the necessary' – i.e., the necessity that preceded regarding the difference between involuntary movement and acquisition. And his statement: 'and nullification' – (in the genitive, coordinated with 'denial') – means: that if there were nothing in the doctrine of Jabr except the establishment of ignorance concerning a matter perceived by necessity, without clashing with the Law, it would be easy; for the worst that follows from it is extreme stupidity and weakness of intellect. How [then] when the doctrine clashes with the Law?! Because the Law has come with the removal of obligation concerning acts in which the servant is not normally capable of being described with their existence or non-existence, and with obligation concerning what is normally feasible for the servant – its doing and leaving – and he has no effect in any of his acts so that the distinction could be valid for us as the Qadariyya claim. Thus nothing remains by which to distinguish between what the Law obligates and what it does not obligate except acquisition (iktisāb) in the sense that preceded in its interpretation, and its absence. If all acts were equal, as the people of Jabr say, then the Law's differentiation between them would be invalidated, and what obligation relies on among them would be invalidated – that is the act which is within the capacity of the obligated one to the exclusion of others. Then no act would be within the capacity of the obligated one normally, so there would be no obligation regarding any of them, due to His statement, exalted: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' [Quran 2:286]. And this is a nullification of the Book, the Sunna, and the consensus of the community. And to this I pointed with my statement: 'And from here it was a heresy' – i.e., because of the necessary consequence of Jabr nullifying the locus of legal obligation and the necessary non-existence of the sign of reward and punishment, it was a heresy affecting the creed of faith.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is a teacher's commentary explaining why the refutation of Jabriyya (determinism) follows from earlier proofs of human acquired capacity (qudra ḥāditha) and the denial of its causal effect. The author first states that the statement 'Thus the doctrine of Jabr is refuted' is a result of two earlier proofs: establishing human capacity and showing it has no effect in its object. He then explains each part of the original statement. The phrase 'because it involves denial of the necessary' refers to the necessary distinction between involuntary movement (ḥarakat al-iḍṭirār) and acquired action (iktisāb). The phrase 'and nullification' (coordinated grammatically) means that even if Jabr only caused ignorance of something known by necessity without contradicting revelation, it would still be intellectual deficiency. But in fact, Jabr contradicts revelation because revelation removes obligation from acts normally beyond the servant's ability (like trembling) and imposes it on acts within the servant's normal ability to do or leave, while the servant has no effect on any of his acts (as the Qadariyya wrongly claim). The only way to distinguish between obligatory and non-obligatory acts is acquisition (iktisāb) in the specific sense defined earlier. If all acts were equal (as Jabr claims), then the Law's differentiation is void, and obligation becomes impossible because no act would be within the servant's capacity, contradicting Quran 2:286. This amounts to nullifying the Quran, Sunna, and consensus. Hence the author called it a heresy affecting the creed of faith.

Key Terms
جبر
قدرة حادثة
اضطرار
اكتساب
تكليف
وسع
بدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Jabriyya only denies human power; but the passage shows it also destroys the basis for divine obligation.
Correction: Jabriyya's denial that acts are within the servant’s capacity erases any distinction between what is obligatory and what is not, thereby nullifying the very possibility of divine command.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the only factor that distinguishes acts which the Law obligates from those it does not?
A) Whether the act is caused by God alone.
B) Whether the servant has acquisition (iktisāb) of the act.
C) Whether the act is possible.
D) Whether the act is a sign for reward.

Key Takeaway
Jabriyya’s doctrine is not merely a philosophical error; it is a heresy because it negates the very possibility of divine obligation by making all human actions involuntary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever find yourself thinking 'it was decreed for me' as an excuse? How does this passage confront that evasion of responsibility by showing that obligation relies on your real capacity to choose otherwise?
?
Are there areas of your life where you feel overburdened spiritually, forgetting that Allah does not burden you beyond your capacity? How can this truth lighten your struggle or deepen your trust?
?
If all your actions were involuntary (as Jabr claims), then reward and punishment would be meaningless. Reflect on a moment you felt truly accountable for a choice—did your conscience affirm the distinction between voluntary and involuntary? What does that tell you about the reality of human responsibility before God?

Refuting Qadariyya: Priority of Divine Power
Proves Qadariyya false because divine power can attach before human power, preventing it would be impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of Qadariyya based on the priority of divine power over human power and the impossibility of human power preventing acts.

Core Idea
Divine power precedes human power, making Qadariyya self-refuting.

This passage continues the refutation of sectarian errors by addressing Qadariyya, who claim humans independently create their acts. Sanusi demonstrates that this leads to a logical impossibility, since divine power can attach to any act before human power does. If human power could prevent that, it would imply impossible consequences, namely making the possible impossible and preponderating the preponderated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the gist of the argument.
Notice the technical terms: qudra qadīma and qudra ḥāditha.
2
Identify the key concepts
Identify 'eternal power' (qudra qadīma) and 'temporal power' (qudra ḥāditha) and understand how they relate to acts.
The eternal power can attach to acts before the temporal power.
3
Trace the logical argument
Follow the conditional: if temporal power were to prevent the act, then what is known to be possible becomes impossible, and the preponderance of the preponderated occurs.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why this argument refutes Qadariyya: since divine power is prior, human power cannot independently create acts without contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومذهب القدريّةِ ( معطوف على قوله : ( مذهب الجبر ) أي : وبطل مذهب القدرية
زيادة تحقيق في إبطال مذهب القدرية ]
ويلزم فيه أيضاً إحالةُ ما عُلِمَ إمكانه ؛ إذ الأفعال يصح تعلُّقُ القدرةِ القديمةِ بها قبل تعلق القدرة الحادثة ، فلو منَعَتها القدرة الحادثةُ للزِمَ ما ذُكِرَ وترجيحُ المرجوح .
الضمير المجرور بـ ( في ( عائد على مذهب القدرية ؛ أي : يلزمُ
His statement: 'And the doctrine of the Qadariyya' – (coordinated with his statement 'the doctrine of Jabr') – i.e., the doctrine of the Qadariyya is also refuted. Further verification on the refutation of the doctrine of the Qadariyya: And there also follows necessarily in it the impossibility of what is known to be possible; for acts are validly related to the eternal power before the temporal power attaches. So if the temporal power prevented them, what was mentioned would follow, and also the preponderance of the preponderated. The pronoun governed by 'in' refers to the doctrine of the Qadariyya; i.e., there follows necessarily.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a brief refutation of Qadariyya (the doctrine that humans independently create their acts). It is coordinated with the previous refutation of Jabr. The author states that the Qadariyya doctrine is also refuted, and then provides a further verification (tahqīq). The argument: The eternal power (qudra qadīma, i.e., divine power) can validly attach to an act before the temporal power (qudra ḥāditha, i.e., human power) attaches. If human power were to prevent the act (by supposedly creating it independently), then it would necessitate the impossibility of something known to be possible (iḥāla mā ‘ulima imkānuhu) and also the preponderance of the preponderated (tarjīḥ al-marjūḥ) – a logical absurdity. The last line clarifies that the pronoun in 'fīhi' refers to the doctrine of Qadariyya, meaning 'there follows necessarily...' (the rest of the argument is cut off in the window). The core idea: Since divine power can attach to any act before human power does, human power cannot prevent it; otherwise, we would have contradictory impossibilities.

Misconception: Qadariyya preserves human free will by claiming humans independently create their acts.
Correction: This passage shows that such a claim leads to logical contradictions: if human power could prevent acts, it would make the possible impossible and cause an absurdity, thus refuting the doctrine.

Key Takeaway

?

Refutation of the Doctrine of Jabr
Sanusi refutes the doctrine of Jabr by showing it nullifies Sharia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the doctrine of Jabr (fatalism) is incompatible with Islamic theology and nullifies the very concept of religious obligation (taklif).

Core Idea
Jabr destroys the foundation of Sharia by making all actions equally involuntary.

Sanusi now takes on the doctrine of Jabr, which claims that all human actions are directly compelled by God with no human agency. In this passage, he demonstrates that such a view has devastating consequences: it obliterates the distinction between obligatory and non-obligatory actions, effectively nullifying the entire structure of Islamic law. Let's follow his logical refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once to get a feel for Sanusi's argumentative style. Don't worry about every word yet.
Notice the use of technical terms like 'iktisāb' and 'wus‘'.
2
Understand the logical premise
Identify the key premise: the only way to distinguish between actions for legal obligation is through acquisition (kasb) within human capacity (wus‘).
This was established earlier in the text.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
See how Sanusi applies the Jabr logic: if all actions are equal (no kasb), then none are within capacity, making obligation impossible.
This leads to contradiction with the Qur'anic verse 2:286.
4
Connect to the broader consequence
Realize that Jabr invalidates the Qur'an, Sunnah, and Ijma, hence it is a dangerous innovation (bid‘a) affecting creed.
Sanusi calls it 'bid‘a mu’aththira fī ‘aqd al-īmān'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عادةً فعله وتركُهُ ، ولا تأثيرَ لهُ في شيءٍ مِنْ أفعالِهِ حتى يصح لنا التفريقُ كما تزعم القدريَّةُ ، فلم يبقَ ما يُفرَّقُ بهِ بينَ مَا يُكَلِّفُ بِهِ الشرعُ وما لا يُكَلِّفُ بهِ .. إلا الاكتساب على المعنى الذي سبق في تفسيره وعدمه . ولو استوَتِ الأفعالُ كلُّها كما يقولُ أهل الجبر .. لبطل تفريق الشرع بينها ، وبطل ما أحالَ عليهِ التكليف منها ؛ وهو الفعلُ الذي في وُسْعَ المكلَّف دونَ غيرِهِ ، وكانَتِ الأفعالُ حينَئِذٍ لا شيء منها في وُسْعِ المكلَّف عادةً ، فلا تكليف إذاً بشيء منها ؛ لقولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ لَا يُكَلِّفُ اللَّهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٨٦] ، وهذا إبطال للكتاب والسنَّةِ وإجماع الأمة ، وإلى هذا أشرتُ بقولي : ( ومن هنا كانَ بدعةً ) أي : ومِنْ أجلِ لزوم إبطال الجبر لمحلَّ التكليف الشرعي ولزوم انتفاء أمارة الثواب والعقاب . . كانَ بدعةً مُؤثرةً في عقدِ الإِيمَانِ
Ordinarily his action and his abandonment, and there is no effect for him in any of his actions so that the distinction becomes valid for us as the Qadariyya claim. So nothing remains by which to distinguish between what the Sharia obliges and what it does not oblige, except acquisition (iktisāb) in the meaning that preceded in its explanation and its absence. And if all actions were equal as the people of Jabr say, then the Sharia's distinction between them would be invalidated, and what obligation relies on from them would be invalidated; that is the action that is within the capacity (wus') of the obligated person (mukallaf) to the exclusion of others. And then actions would be such that none of them would be within the capacity of the obligated person ordinarily, so there would be no obligation for any of them, due to His saying, the Exalted: 'Allah does not burden a soul except with its capacity' [Al-Baqarah: 286]. And this is an invalidation of the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus of the Umma. And to this I referred in my saying: 'And from here it was a bid'a (innovation),' meaning: because of the necessary invalidation of Jabr for the domain of legal obligation and the necessary absence of the indication of reward and punishment... it was an innovation affecting the creed of faith.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine of Jabr (fatalism). The author argues that if all human actions are equally predetermined by God without any human agency, then there is no criterion to distinguish between actions that are obligatory and those that are not. The only possible distinction would be acquisition (kasb), but if all actions are equal as the Jabrites claim, then none are within human capacity, thus nullifying the very basis of religious obligation (taklif). The author supports this with the Qur'anic verse 'Allah does not burden a soul except with its capacity,' which implies that taklif requires capacity. Therefore, Jabr contradicts the Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus of the umma, making it a harmful innovation affecting the creed of faith.

Correction: Sanusi demonstrates that Jabr logically destroys the basis of Sharia and contradicts clear Qur'anic verses, consensus, and the Sunnah, making it a harmful innovation.

Key Takeaway

How does this lesson remind you that your wus' (capacity) is precisely what makes obedience meaningful? What one act of obedience will you undertake today as a conscious act of acquisition (iktisab) rather than passive submission?

Two Objections Against Qadariyya: The Possible and the Preference
Sanusi presents two objections against Qadariyya: the return of the possible to impossible and tarjih al-marjuh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical objections that Sanusi raises against the Qadariyya doctrine, especially the argument that their view makes the possible impossible.

Core Idea
If human power can block divine power, the possible becomes impossible.

Sanusi has already presented one argument against Qadariyya from the proof of mutual prevention. Here he adds two more powerful objections. The first shows that the Qadariyya claim leads to a contradiction: what was possible for God becomes impossible. The second objection, tarjīḥ al-marjūḥ, is briefly mentioned. In this lesson, we will examine the first objection closely, including a potential counterargument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument, then read the English translation to ensure understanding.
Notice how Sanusi first states the two objections and then explains the first in detail.
2
Identify the two objections
Look for the phrase 'one is the necessity of the possible returning as impossible; the second is the preference of the weaker' and note how they are introduced.
These are additional to the previous argument from the proof of mutual prevention.
3
Trace the first objection's logic
Follow the steps: the action is possible before human power is created; God has power over all possible things; when human power is created, the Qadariyya claim it becomes impossible for God; therefore, the possible becomes impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Examine the counterargument and refutation
The counterargument says the impossibility is accidental. Sanusi refutes it by arguing that human power cannot be a valid cause to prevent divine power, so the impossibility must be essential, which is untenable.
Focus on why the accidental impossibility claim fails.
5
Understand the second objection
The second objection (tarjīḥ al-marjūḥ) is mentioned but not elaborated. It means preferring the weaker over the stronger, which is irrational.
This objection is also a logical consequence of the Qadariyya view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومذهب القدريّةِ ( معطوف على قوله : ( مذهب الجبر ) أي : وبطل مذهب القدرية
زيادة تحقيق في إبطال مذهب القدرية ]
ويلزم فيه أيضاً إحالةُ ما عُلِمَ إمكانه ؛ إذ الأفعال يصح تعلُّقُ القدرةِ القديمةِ بها قبل تعلق القدرة الحادثة ، فلو منَعَتها القدرة الحادثةُ للزِمَ ما ذُكِرَ وترجيحُ المرجوح .
الضمير المجرور بـ ( في ( عائد على مذهب القدرية ؛ أي : يلزم
فيه محذوران آخرانِ زيادةً على ما لزمهم مِنْ عجز القدرةِ القديمة على ما سبق في دليل التمانعِ : أحدُهُما : لزوم عود الممكن مستحيلاً الثاني : ترجيح المرجوح
وتقرير الأول : أنْ نقولَ : فعلُ العبدِ قبلَ أَنْ تُخلَقَ لهُ القدرة الحادثة ممكن ، وكلُّ ممكن فهو مقدور للباري جلَّ وعلا ، فينتجُ : فعلُ
العبدِ مقدورٌ للباري تعالى ؛ فإذا خلق الله سبحانه للعبد قدرةً حادثة . . قالَ القدريَّةُ : إِنَّهُ يزولُ حينَئِذٍ عن الفعلِ ما ثبتَ لَهُ مِنْ إمكانِ أَنْ يُوجِدَ بالقدرة القديمة ، وصارَ إذ ذاك مستحيل الوجودِ بها ، فقد لزمَ أَنَّ ما كانَ
ممكناً باعتبار القدرةِ القديمةِ صارَ مستحيلاً بالنسبة إليها !
لا يُقالُ : استحالته عارضة لسبب ؛ وهو تعلق القدرة الحادثة بهِ ، فاستحال أنْ يكونَ الفعلُ موجوداً بقدرتين ، والاستحالة العارضة لا تقدح في الإمكان الذاتي لأنَّا نقولُ : لم يظهر لهذهِ الاستحالة سبب يصح ، فتعيَّنَ على زعمهم أن تكونَ ذاتيَّة ؛ لأنَّ القدرة الحادثة التي جعلوها مانعةً مِنْ تعلق القدرة القديمة بالفعل لا تصلحُ أن تكونَ مانعةً مِنْ ذلكَ ، بل الذي يصلح عقلاً ونقلاً العكس .
His saying: 'And the doctrine of the Qadariyya' is conjuncted to his saying: 'The doctrine of Jabr,' meaning: the doctrine of Qadariyya is also invalidated. Additional verification on the invalidation of the doctrine of Qadariyya. And it also necessitates in it the impossibility of what is known to be possible; because actions are such that the eternal power (qudra qadīma) can attach to them before the created power (qudra ḥāditha) attaches. So if the created power prevents them, then what was mentioned would be necessary, and the preference of the weaker (tarjīḥ al-marjūḥ) [would occur]. The pronoun in the genitive with 'in' refers to the doctrine of Qadariyya; i.e., there follow from it two additional objections besides what was necessary for them from the incapacity of the eternal power as preceded in the proof of mutual prevention (dalīl al-tamānu'): one is the necessity of the possible returning as impossible; the second is the preference of the weaker. And the explanation of the first: that we say: The action of the servant before the created power is created for him is possible, and every possible is within the power of the Creator, may He be glorified and exalted. Thus, it follows that the action of the servant is within the power of the Creator, the Exalted. So when Allah, glory be to Him, creates for the servant a created power, the Qadariyya say that then what was established for the action from the possibility of being brought into existence by the eternal power disappears, and it becomes then impossible of existence by it. So it becomes necessary that what was possible with respect to the eternal power becomes impossible with respect to it! It cannot be said: Its impossibility is accidental due to a cause, which is the attachment of the created power to it, so it is impossible for the action to exist by two powers, and accidental impossibility does not vitiate essential possibility. Because we say: This impossibility does not have a valid cause, so on their assumption it must be essential; because the created power which they made as a preventer from the attachment of the eternal power to the action is not suitable to be a preventer from that; rather, what is suitable, both intellectually and textually, is the opposite.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage presents two logical objections against the Qadariyya doctrine that human actions are produced solely by human power independent of divine power. The first objection is that if human power can prevent divine power from acting, then what was possible for God to do becomes impossible, which is a contradiction. The second objection is the 'preference of the weaker' (tarjīḥ al-marjūḥ), i.e., preferring the weaker or less likely over the stronger. The author elaborates on the first objection: before human power is created, the action is possible and within God's power. If, after human power is created, the action becomes impossible for God, then the same entity changes from possible to impossible, which is absurd. He then addresses a potential counterargument that the impossibility is accidental due to the attachment of human power. He refutes this by arguing that human power cannot be a valid cause for preventing divine power, so the impossibility must be essential, which is unacceptable. Thus, the Qadariyya position leads to logical inconsistency.

قدرية
قدرة قديمة
قدرة حادثة
إمكان
استحالة
ترجيح المرجوح
عارضة
ذاتي
دليل التمانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That human power and divine power can coexist without conflict, such that human actions are solely under human control.
Correction: This passage shows that if human power could prevent divine power, then divine power would be limited, leading to logical absurdities like the possible becoming impossible. Thus, human actions must be dependent on divine power.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the consequence of the Qadariyya claim that human power can prevent divine power?
A) It proves human free will.
B) It leads to the impossibility of the possible and the preference of the weaker.
C) It establishes a middle path.
D) It is consistent with divine justice.

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya doctrine leads to the absurd conclusion that what is possible for God becomes impossible, and that the weaker preference outweighs the stronger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you act, do you genuinely believe that your power is entirely dependent on God's power, or do you feel a sense of independent capability? How does this lesson challenge that feeling?
?
Sanusi argues that if your power could block God's power, then the possible becomes impossible. Pause and ask yourself: In your daily life, do you ever act as if your choices and abilities are independent of God's will, even momentarily? What would it mean to live with constant awareness that God's power is never blocked by yours?
?
The Qadariyya thought that human power could prevent divine power. In your heart, do you sometimes fear that your sins or failures are beyond God's control or forgiveness? How does this logical argument remind you of the absolute supremacy of God's power over all your actions?
