Second Objection: Preponderating the Less Likely
Sanusi states the second objection is apparent, then presents and refutes a Qadariyya argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection (tarjīḥ al-marjūḥ) raised by the Ashʿarīs, the Qadariyya's counterargument, and the two refutations provided by the author.

Core Idea
The second objection is logically apparent and the Qadariyya's response is self-defeating.

This brief but dense passage presents the second of three objections against the Ashʿarī position. Sanusi quickly declares it apparent, then introduces a Qadariyya argument and refutes it with two pointed criticisms. The discussion hinges on the relationship between God's power and created human power, and the principle of al-aṣlaḥ (most beneficial).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the first sentence states the objection is 'apparent'. Then there is a marginal notation, followed by an argument from the Qadariyya and the refutation.
The passage is fragmented; the line beginning with '1' may be a marginal note or heading.
2
Identify the Qadariyya argument
The Qadariyya claim that God has always had power over human acts by removing the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha). Understand what 'removing the originated power' means in this context.
This argument tries to preserve God's omnipotence while denying human free will.
3
Analyze the first refutation
The author responds: if God removes the originated power, then in the presence of that power, God would not have power over the act. This creates a contradiction: God cannot both have power and not have power over the same act.
This refutation exposes a logical flaw in the Qadariyya position.
4
Analyze the second refutation
The author invokes the Qadariyya's own principle of al-aṣlaḥ (observing what is most beneficial for the servant). Removing the originated power after imposing obligations (taklīf) would be harmful, contradicting that principle.
This refutation shows internal inconsistency within the Qadariyya school.
5
Consider the overall lesson
Reflect on how Sanusi uses both logical and doctrinal arguments to refute an opponent's position. Note that the second objection is not elaborated because it is considered self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني - وهو ترجيح المرجوح - : فهو ظاهر
1 محالات لازمةٌ عن مذهب القدرية ]
قالوا : لم يزل يقدر عليها ؛ بأنْ يَسلُبَ القدرة الحادثة . قلنا : فقد لزم إذاً ألا يقدر عليها مع وجودِ القدرة الحادثة ! وأيضاً : مِنْ أصلِكُم وجـوب مـراعـاة الأصلح
ولا يمكن سلبها عندكم بعد التكليف
As for the second aspect – and it is the preponderating of the less likely – it is apparent. 1. Necessary impossibilities from the doctrine of the Qadariyya. They said: He never ceased to have power over them, by removing the originated power. We said: then it necessarily follows that He does not have power over them in the presence of the originated power! And also: from your own principle is the obligation of observing the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ). And it is not possible to remove it according to you after the obligation (taklīf).
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage introduces the second objection, 'preponderating the less likely' (tarjīḥ al-marjūḥ), but says it is apparent (it is obvious why it is a problem). Then there is what appears to be a marginal note or separate argument: 'Necessary impossibilities from the doctrine of the Qadariyya.' The Qadariyya are reported to say: God always had power over human acts, meaning He could remove the originated power (so that He retains full control). The author refutes this by saying: if that were so, then in the presence of the originated power, God would not have power over those acts—which contradicts the claim that He never ceases to have power. Also, from the Qadariyya's own principle of observing the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ), they cannot remove the originated power after the servant has been charged with obligations (taklīf), because that would be against the servant's best interest. The argument is brief and incomplete, but it points to the incoherence of the Qadariyya position.

Key Terms
ترجيح المرجوح
القدرة الحادثة
الأصلح
تكليف
سلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Qadariyya argument (God always has power by removing human power) is coherent and does not lead to contradiction.
Correction: The author shows that if God removes the originated power, then during the time that power exists, God lacks power over the act, contradicting the claim of eternal power. Additionally, it conflicts with the principle of al-aṣlaḥ.

Checkpoint
According to the author's refutation, what follows if God always has power over acts by removing the originated power?
A) He has power both with and without the originated power
B) He does not have power in the presence of the originated power
C) The act becomes impossible
D) The originated power is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
The second objection is that the Ashʿarī position leads to preponderating the less likely, but the Qadariyya's response is invalid because removing originated power would negate God's power in its presence and contradicts their own principle of al-aṣlaḥ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever justify it by thinking that God's power over your action somehow makes Him responsible, not you? How does Sanusi's refutation of the Qadariyya expose the error of shifting blame to God? What would it mean for you to fully accept that your choices are real and accountable before Him starting today?
?
Sanusi argues that removing human power after imposing obligations would be harmful and contradicts God's wisdom. Have you ever doubted God's wisdom in allowing you to struggle with a command while feeling weak? In what area of your life are you tempted to believe that God's giving you a command is not matched by His giving you the ability? How will you trust Him to provide what you need to obey, rather than expecting Him to remove your responsibility?

Refutation of Tarjīḥ al-Marjūḥ
Refutes the Qadariyya objection about God's ability to remove originated power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash‘arī response to the Qadariyya claim that God can remove originated power, and the two arguments used to refute it.

Core Idea
God's ability to remove power does not disprove human acquisition; it would make human power meaningless.

This passage addresses a key Qadariyya objection to the Ash‘arī proof from mutual exclusivity. The Qadariyya argue that God could always have power over human acts by removing the originated power. The Ash‘arī response shows that this claim leads to absurdity and contradicts the Qadariyya's own principle of al-aṣlaḥ. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the logical flow: objection, first reply, second reply.
The passage ends with a cross-reference to an earlier fuller treatment.
2
Identify the Qadariyya objection
The Qadariyya claim: God has always been able to perform human acts by removing the originated power from the servant. Understand what 'preferring the weaker' (tarjīḥ al-marjūḥ) means in this context.
Tarjīḥ al-marjūḥ means giving preference to the weaker possibility, which they consider absurd.
3
Analyze the first reply
The Ash‘arī replies: If God removes the originated power while it exists, then the servant would not be able to act even when the power is present—a clear contradiction.
This shows that the Qadariyya claim leads to a logical impossibility.
4
Analyze the second reply
The second argument uses the Qadariyya's own principle of al-aṣlaḥ (God must do what is best). Removing power after imposing obligation (taklīf) would not be al-aṣlaḥ, so they cannot accept it.
This turns their own doctrine against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني - وهو ترجيح المرجوح - : فهو ظاهر
1 محالات لازمةٌ عن مذهب القدرية
قالوا : لم يزل يقدر عليها ؛ بأنْ يَسلُبَ القدرة الحادثة . قلنا : فقد لزم إذاً ألا يقدر عليها مع وجودِ القدرة الحادثة ! وأيضاً : مِنْ أصلِكُم وجـوب مـراعـاة الأصلح
ولا يمكن سلبها عندكم بعد التكليف
قد تقدم تقرير هاذا الذي أجابوا بهِ وتقرير ردِّهِ أكمل تقريرٍ في شرح قولنا : ( وبهذا الدليل بعينه ؛ أعني : دليل التمانع . . . ) المسألةَ فانظره هناك
As for the second face—which is preferring the weaker (tarjīḥ al-marjūḥ)—it is clear. 1 Necessary absurdities following from the doctrine of the Qadariyya. They said: 'He has always been able to do them, by removing the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha).' We said: 'Then it necessarily follows that He is not able to do them in the presence of the originated power!' Also: from your own principle is the obligation of observing what is most appropriate (al-aṣlaḥ), and it is not possible to remove it after taklīf according to you. The explanation of this that they answered with, and the explanation of its refutation, have already been presented in the fullest explanation in our commentary on our statement: 'And by this same proof, namely the proof of mutual exclusivity (dalīl al-tamānuʿ)...' the matter—so look at it there.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the second line of argument (al-wajh al-thānī) called 'preferring the weaker' (tarjīḥ al-marjūḥ). The Qadariyya object: God could always have power over human acts by removing the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) from the servant. The Ash‘arī replies: If God removes the originated power while it exists, then that would mean the servant is not able to act even when the power is present—a contradiction. Then the author adds a second argument: According to the Qadariyya's own principle of al-aṣlaḥ (that God must do what is best for His servants), removing the power after imposing obligation (taklīf) would not be al-aṣlaḥ, so they cannot accept this. The passage ends with a cross-reference to an earlier fuller treatment of this argument in the commentary on the proof of tamānuʿ.

أصلح
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is exclusively Ash‘arī and not based on the opponent's premises.
Correction: It is actually based on the Qadariyya's own principle of al-aṣlaḥ, turning their doctrine against them.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash‘arī reply, what problematic consequence follows from the Qadariyya's claim that God can remove the originated power?
A) God would be unable to create the act.
B) The servant would be unable to act even while having power.
C) The act would become involuntary.
D) God's power would be limited.

Key Takeaway
The Ash‘arī response shows that the Qadariyya objection self-destructs because it would negate the very power they affirm, and contradicts their own principle of al-aṣlaḥ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely on your own abilities and forget that God could remove them at any moment? How would your daily actions change if you truly lived with the awareness that your power is borrowed and could be taken away?
?
When you face a difficult choice, do you assume that God must give you the ability to do what is best? What if His wisdom withholds power for a reason you cannot see? Are you willing to trust Him even when your own strength seems insufficient?
?
Is there an area of your life where you claim to be powerless, yet you have not sincerely called upon God to grant you the ability? What would it look like to take real responsibility while acknowledging that all power comes from Him?

Acts as Signs of Reward and Punishment
Sanusi refutes the Qadariyya argument about reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to the Qadariyya objection that reward and punishment require human causal power, and how acts function as legal indicators (amārāt shar‘iyya) of God's decree.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment are not caused by acts; acts are signs.

The Qadariyya argued that if God decrees all actions, then human reward or punishment would be unjust because humans lack real causal power. Sanusi presents a powerful Ash'ari rebuttal: reward and punishment are not directly caused by acts; rather, acts are legal indicators (amārāt shar‘iyya) that God creates to signal His intention about a person's final outcome. This lesson unpacks that response and the logical entailment the Qadariyya relied on.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: a heading, an objection, a response, a verse, a supplication, and then a formal sophism (shubha) with its entailment.
The passage combines creedal assertion with logical argument.
2
Identify the Qadariyya objection
Find the sentence where the Qadariyya ask: 'How can He reward or punish him for something that is not his act?' This is the core challenge. Note that they assume reward/punishment must be tied to the servant's own causal power.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
The response states that reward and punishment are not 'mu‘allal' (caused) by acts; rather, acts are 'amārāt shar‘iyya' — legal signs. The verse from Hūd is cited to show that God could have made all people one community but did not, indicating His will in differentiating outcomes.
The term 'ghayr mu‘allalīn' is crucial: it denies necessary causation between act and reward/punishment.
4
Analyze the formal shubha
The passage then presents the Qadariyya's sophism as a formal argument: if the servant's power has no effect, reward/punishment would be invalid; but reward/punishment are valid, so the power must have effect. The entailment (luzūm) is that without servant power, acts would be indistinguishable from accidental properties or even the servant's essence, which is absurd.
This sets up an Ash'ari resolution via the concept of kasb (acquisition), though not fully developed here.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this refutation preserves divine justice without requiring human causal power. Acts remain morally significant because God assigns them as signs, not because humans create them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعمالنا أعلام الثواب والعقاب
قالوا : فكيف يثيبه أو يعاقبه على غيرِ فعلِهِ ؟! قلنا : يفعل ما شاءَ ، لا يُسأَل عما يفعل ، والثواب والعقاب غيرُ مُعلَّلين ، وإِنَّما الأفعال أمارات شرعيَّةٌ عليهما ؛ يخلق الله تعالى منها في كلِّ مُكلَّف ما يدلُّ شرعاً على ما أرادَ بهِ في عُقباه، فكل ميسر لما خُلِقَ لَهُ ، وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَجَعَلَ النَّاسَ أُمَةً وَاحِدَةً ﴾ [هود : ۱۱۸] ، نسأله سبحانه حسْنَ الخاتمة بفضلِهِ
هذهِ شبهةٌ مِنْ شبهات القدريَّةِ ، وتقريرها : أن قالوا : لو لم يكن لقدرة العبد تأثير في فعلِهِ .. لَمَا صح أنْ يُثاب عليه أو يُعاقَبَ ، والتالي معلوم البطلان ، فالمقدَّمُ مثلُه .
وبيان الملازمة : أنَّ الفعل إذا لم يكن أثراً لقدرةِ العبدِ صارَ لا فرق بينه وبين ألوانِهِ ، بل لا فرقَ بينَهُ وبينَ ذاتِهِ وسائرِ ذواتِ العالم
Our works are signs of reward and punishment. They said: 'How can He reward or punish him for something that is not his act?' We said: 'He does as He wills, He is not asked about what He does, and reward and punishment are not caused (ghayr mu‘allalīn); rather, acts are legal indicators (amārāt shar‘iyya) of them; God creates from them in every obligated person what indicates, according to law, what He intends for him in his end. So everyone is facilitated for what he was created for. Had your Lord willed, He would have made mankind one community [Hūd 11:118]. We ask Him— exalted is He—for a good end by His grace.' This is a sophism (shubha) from the sophisms of the Qadariyya. Its formulation: they said: 'If the servant's power had no effect in his act, it would not be valid to reward or punish him for it.' The consequent is known to be false, so the antecedent is likewise false. The explanation of the entailment: If the act is not an effect of the servant's power, then there is no distinction between it and his colors—rather, no distinction between it and his essence and the rest of the essences of the world.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes a Qadariyya objection about reward and punishment. It begins with the heading 'Our works are signs of reward and punishment' (a‘lām al-thawāb wa al-‘iqāb). The Qadariyya ask: How can God reward or punish for an act that is not the servant’s own doing? The Ash‘arī replies that God does as He wills, and reward and punishment are not caused (mu‘allal) by the act in a natural sense; rather, acts are legal indicators (amārāt shar‘iyya) created by God to show His intention regarding the servant’s final outcome. The verse from Hūd is cited: 'Had your Lord willed, He would have made mankind one community.' The author then identifies this as a formal shubha (sophism) from the Qadariyya: they argue that if the servant’s power has no effect, reward/punishment would be invalid, but reward/punishment are valid, so the power must have effect. The author then explains the entailment (luzūm): if the act were not an effect of the servant’s power, there would be no distinction between the act and the servant’s colors (accidental properties) or even his essence—implying absurdity. The argument is left incomplete in this passage; it likely presupposes an Ash‘arī response on the basis of kasb (acquisition).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position denies moral responsibility because humans have no real causal power.
Correction: The passage shows that responsibility is based on legal signs (amārāt shar‘iyya) that God creates, not on human causal efficacy. Acts remain morally significant as indicators of one's final outcome.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash‘arī response, what are acts in relation to reward and punishment?
A) They are the efficient causes of reward and punishment.
B) They are legal indicators (amārāt shar‘iyya) of God's decree.
C) They are irrelevant to reward and punishment.
D) They are the servant's own creation.

Key Takeaway
Acts are not causes of reward or punishment but legal signs of God's decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you see it as something you produced or as a sign from God? How would that shift your reliance on yourself versus on His grace?
?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment. If your acts are merely signs, not causes, of His reward—does that change how you seek forgiveness for your shortcomings? Will you still plead your good deeds, or will you throw yourself on His mercy alone?
?
The Qadariyya thought they could justify moral responsibility through human power. Are there areas in your life where you try to 'earn' Allah's pleasure by your own strength, forgetting that even your ability to act is a sign of His will? What would it look like to live each moment as a sign, not a product, of His decree?

The Qadariyya Objection and Sunni Response on Reward and Punishment
Sanusi presents and refutes the Qadariyya objection regarding reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qadariyya objection that predestination makes reward and punishment unjust, and the Sunni response that acts are merely indicators (amarat) of God's will, not causes.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment are based on divine wisdom, not human causal power.

This passage presents a classic theological exchange between the Qadariyya and Ahl al-Sunnah. The Qadariyya argue that if human actions are not caused by human power, then reward and punishment become meaningless. The Sunni response denies this implication and redefines the role of actions as indicators of divine grace or abandonment.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Notice that Sanusi starts with the Sunni thesis: 'Our deeds are signs of reward and punishment.' This sets the stage for the entire discussion.
This is the conclusion they will defend.
2
Understand the Qadariyya objection
The Qadariyya argue that if human power has no effect on actions, then actions are like colors or other involuntary things. Since we are not rewarded or punished for those, we should not be for actions either.
They rely on a logical implication (mulazama).
3
See how Ahl al-Sunnah deny the implication
They accept that human power has no effect, but deny that this prevents reward or punishment. Reward and punishment are not causally necessitated; they are based on divine will.
The key concept is that acts are 'amarat' (indicators).
4
Reflect on the verse and closing prayer
The passage ends with Quranic support (Hud 11:118) and a supplication, reminding us of ultimate reliance on God.
This shows the theological and devotional dimensions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعمالنا أعلام الثواب والعقاب ] قالوا : فكيف يثيبه أو يعاقبه على غيرِ فعلِهِ ؟! قلنا : يفعل ما شاءَ ، لا يُسأَل عما يفعل ، والثواب والعقاب غيرُ مُعلَّلين ، وإِنَّما الأفعال أمارات شرعيَّةٌ عليهما ؛ يخلق الله تعالى منها في كلِّ مُكلَّف ما يدلُّ شرعاً على ما أرادَ بهِ في عُقباه، فكل ميسر لما خُلِقَ لَهُ ، وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَجَعَلَ النَّاسَ أُمَةً وَاحِدَةً ﴾ [هود : ١١٨] ، نسأله سبحانه حسْنَ الخاتمة بفضلِهِ
هذهِ شبهةٌ مِنْ شبهات القدريَّةِ ، وتقريرها : أن قالوا : لو لم يكن لقدرة العبد تأثير في فعلِهِ .. لَمَا صح أنْ يُثاب عليه أو يُعاقَبَ ، والتالي معلوم البطلان ، فالمقدَّمُ مثلُه .
وبيان الملازمة : أنَّ الفعل إذا لم يكن أثراً لقدرةِ العبدِ صارَ لا فرق بينه وبين ألوانِهِ ، بل لا فرقَ بينَهُ وبينَ ذاتِهِ وسائرِ ذواتِ العالم وأعراضه ، بجامعِ أَنَّ الجميعَ لا أثر له فيه ، فكما أَنَّهُ لا يُتاب ولا يُعاقَبُ على وجودِ ألوانه ووجودِ ذاتِهِ ووجود سائر أجزاء العالم وأعراضه ؛ لكونه لا تأثيرَ لهُ في شيءٍ مِنْ ذلك .. كذلك يلزم ألا يُتابَ ولا يُعاقَبَ على شيءٍ مِنْ أعمالِهِ ؛ لأنَّهُ أيضاً لا تأثيرَ لهُ في شيءٍ منها أصلاً .
أجابَ أهلُ السنَّةِ رضي الله عنهم بمنع الملازمة
•
قولهم في بيانها : إِنَّ الفعل حينئذٍ يصيرُ كاللون وغيرِهِ ممَّا لا تأثير للقدرة الحادثة فيه أصلاً
قلنا : هو كذلك عندَنا مِنْ غيرِ فرقٍ
•
قولهم : فيلزمُ ألا يُثاب عليهِ ولا يُعاقَبَ كما لا يُثاب ولا يُعاقَبُ على الألوان ونحوها ... فعلاً
قلنا : لا ملازمة بين الثواب والعقاب وبين كون سببه للمُكلَّف ، كيف وقد علمتُم مِنْ مذهب خصومكم أنَّ الله تعالى أنْ يُعاقِبَ البريء ويعطي إنعامه للمذنب العاصي ، يفعل ما يشاء ؟! والأفعالُ الواقعة على يد العبدِ أمارات وضعها الشارع على السعادة والشقاوة ، ولو وضعَ غيرَها مِنَ الألوان والطعوم ونحوها أماراتٍ عليهما .. لكانَتْ صالحةً لذلك ، وليسَ للثواب والعقاب علَّةٌ عقليَّةٌ تقتضيهما ، وكلُّ ما أُطلِقَ عليهِ في الشرع أنَّه سبب لهما . . فإنَّما المراد بالسبب الأمارة ، وقع التسامح بالتعبير عنها بالسبب ؛ إذ لا مشاحة في الألفاظ اللغويَّةِ إِذا فُهِمَتِ المقاصد منها
Our deeds are signs of reward and punishment. They said: How can He reward or punish him for something not his act? We said: He does what He wills, He is not asked about what He does, and reward and punishment are not caused by reasons; rather deeds are legal indicators of them; Allah creates from them in every responsible person what legally indicates what He intended for him in his end, so everyone is facilitated for what he was created for, and if your Lord had willed, He would have made mankind one community [Hud 11:118]. We ask Him for a good end by His grace. This is an objection (shubha) from the objections of the Qadariyya, and its explanation: they said: If the power of the servant had no effect in his act, then it would not be valid for him to be rewarded or punished for it, and the consequent is known to be false, so the antecedent is likewise. And the demonstration of the implication: that when the act is not an effect of the servant's power, no distinction remains between it and his colors, rather no distinction between it and his essence and the rest of the essences of the world and their accidents, by the common factor that all have no effect in it; so just as he is not rewarded or punished for the existence of his colors, the existence of his essence, and the existence of the rest of the parts of the world and their accidents, because he has no effect in any of that, so likewise it would be necessary that he not be rewarded or punished for any of his acts, because he also has no effect in any of them at all. Ahl al-Sunnah (may Allah be pleased with them) responded by denying the implication. Their statement in its explanation: The act then becomes like color and other things in which the originated power has no effect at all. We said: It is so for us without any difference. Their statement: So it would necessitate that he not be rewarded or punished for it just as he is not rewarded or punished for colors and the like ... indeed. We said: There is no necessary connection between reward and punishment and their being a cause for the responsible person. How? You have known from the school of your opponents that Allah can punish the innocent and give His favor to the guilty sinner; He does what He wills. The acts occurring by the hand of the servant are indicators (amarat) placed by the Lawgiver on felicity and misery. If He had placed other things like colors, tastes, and the like as indicators of them, they would have been valid for that. There is no rational cause necessitating reward and punishment. Whatever is called a cause (sabab) for them in the Law is only meant as an indicator; there is looseness in expression by calling it a cause, for there is no quibbling over linguistic terms when the intended meanings are understood.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (shubha wa jawab) between the Qadariyya (those who affirm human causal power) and Ahl al-Sunnah. The passage begins with a statement: 'Our deeds are signs of reward and punishment'—a summary of the Sunni position. Then an interlocutor objects: How can God reward or punish a person for an act that is not truly his? The Sunni reply: God does as He wills and is not questioned; reward and punishment are not based on rational causes; rather deeds are legal indicators (amarat) that God creates in each person to indicate His intent for them in the afterlife. This is supported by the verse 'Everyone is facilitated for what he was created for' and the quote from Hud 11:118. The author then introduces the full shubha from the Qadariyya: If human power has no effect in the act, then there is no difference between that act and involuntary things like color, essence, or other accidents. Since a person is not rewarded or punished for those involuntary things, he should not be rewarded or punished for his actions either. The consequent (no reward/punishment) is false, so the antecedent (no effect) must be false. Ahl al-Sunnah respond by denying the implication (man' al-mulazama). They accept that indeed there is no effect of human power—acts are like colors in that respect—but deny that this leads to the impossibility of reward/punishment. Their reasoning: There is no necessary link between reward/punishment and the act being a causal product of the servant. Even the opponents (e.g., the Ash'ari? or other groups) accept that God can punish the innocent and reward the guilty; He does as He wills. What matters is that acts are merely indicators (amarat) placed by the Lawgiver on felicity or misery. If God had made colors or tastes as indicators, they would serve the same purpose. Reward and punishment are not rational necessities; any 'cause' mentioned in the Law is actually an indicator, and such looseness in terminology is acceptable as long as the intended meaning is clear.

Key Terms
أعلام
أمارات
قدرة العبد
ثواب وعقاب
ملازمة
شبهة
قدريَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sunni view that human actions are not caused by human power makes reward and punishment arbitrary or unjust.
Correction: The passage explains that reward and punishment are not based on rational necessity but on divine wisdom; acts are merely indicators placed by God to signify His decree.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunni response in this passage, what is the relationship between human actions and reward or punishment?
A) Human actions are the efficient causes of reward and punishment.
B) Human actions are merely legal indicators (amarat) that God has placed on felicity or misery.
C) There is no relationship; reward and punishment are completely disconnected from actions.
D) Human actions determine reward and punishment by their intrinsic moral value.

Key Takeaway
Acts are not causes of reward or punishment; they are divine indicators of what God has willed for each person.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your deeds do not cause your reward or punishment but are merely signs of what God has decreed for you, how does this change the way you view your own efforts in worship? Are you tempted to rely on your actions, or do you truly depend on God's grace? What would it mean for you to live with the constant awareness that your final outcome is entirely in God's hands?
?
Consider the Qadariyya objection: they thought it unjust for God to punish for an act the servant did not cause. Have you ever felt that God's decree was unfair because you 'tried your best'? Based on this lesson, how would you answer your own heart when it questions God's justice? Will you submit to His will even when you do not see a rational link between your actions and His judgment?
?
The passage says that if God had chosen colors or tastes as indicators of felicity, they would have been valid. This means that your salvation is not tied to the intrinsic value of your deeds but to God's free choice. Does this realization make you more humble or more complacent? What must change in your daily life to reflect true reliance on God's grace rather than on your own performance?

The Servant is Compelled in the Form of the Free
Response to the objection that praise and blame require free choice and human creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response to the objection that divine predestination makes praise and blame unjust by affirming that human actions are ultimately from Allah while preserving moral accountability.

Core Idea
The servant is compelled, but in a mold of choice—actions are ascribed to Allah yet we are responsible.

This passage addresses a key objection against the Ash'ari position on human acts. If Allah creates all actions, how can humans be praised or blamed? The response clarifies that while actions are necessitated by divine power, the signs of choice are present, making moral judgment valid. The author also invokes the creation of motive and power and Allah's eternal knowledge to undermine the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the opponents argue that if humans do not truly perform actions, then praise and blame are unjust, and they cite Quran 4:165 to support their point.
Notice the strength of the objection—it appeals to both reason and revelation.
2
Examine the first response
The author briefly refers to an earlier point ('from the meaning of what preceded it'). This reminds us that the entailment (that blame requires human creation) has already been denied.
This is a concise reference; the commentary will explain more.
3
Consider the two additional refutations
The author then provides two independent refutations: (1) the creation of motive (dā'ī) and power (qudra) means divine causation is complete, and (2) Allah's eternal knowledge encompasses all, so nothing occurs outside His will.
These refutations show that the objection fails on multiple fronts.
4
Grasp the positive thesis
The core statement: 'the servant is compelled in a mold of choice'—this means that although actions are ultimately determined by Allah, the servant experiences choice and is held accountable.
This is the key Ash'ari doctrine of kasb (acquisition).
5
Reflect on moral accountability
Ask yourself: How does this view allow for praise and blame if actions are compelled? The passage says 'it is fitting to consider both matters'—i.e., both divine determination and human responsibility are maintained.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : كيفَ يُمدَحُ العبدُ أو يُذَمُّ على غيرِ ما فعل ؟! ويلزمُ أنْ يكونَ للعبادِ الحجَّةُ في الآخرة ، وقد قال تعالى : لِئَلَّا يَكُونَ لِلنَّاسِ عَلَى اللَّهِ حُجَّةُ بَعدَ الرُّسُلِ [النساء : ١٦٥] .
قلنا : من معنى ما قبله (۱)
وأيضاً : يبطلُ بمسألة خلْقِ الداعي والقدرة ، وبعلمه القديم المحيط بكل شيء
والحقُّ : أنَّ العبد مجبورٌ في قالب مختارِ ، فَحَسُنَ فِيهِ رَعْيُ الأمرين على تقديرِ تسليم أصل التحسين والتقبيح العقليين .
They said: How is the servant praised or blamed for what he did not do?! And it would necessitate that servants have an argument against Allah on the Day of Judgment, while He said: 'Lest people have an argument against Allah after the messengers' [Quran 4:165]. We said: From the meaning of what preceded it. And also: It is invalidated by the issue of the creation of the motive (al-dā'ī) and power, and by His eternal knowledge encompassing everything. And the truth is that the servant is compelled in a mold of choice. So it is fitting in him to consider both matters, on the assumption of accepting the principle of rational good and evil (taḥsīn wa taqbīḥ 'aqliyyayn).
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response. The objector argues that if humans do not truly perform actions, then praise and blame are unjust, and humans would have a valid argument against Allah. The response first refers to the previous answer (denying the entailment). Then it adds two additional refutations: the first is based on the divine creation of the motive (al-dā'ī) and power (al-qudra) – since Allah creates these, the action is attributable to Him. The second is Allah's eternal knowledge which encompasses all things. Then the author states the true position: the servant is compelled (majbūr) in a mold of choice (qālib mukhtār). This means that while human acts are ultimately necessitated by divine decree, the appearance of choice is preserved, allowing for moral accountability. This is sound if one accepts the Ash'ari position that good and evil are not rational but revealed (or if one accepts rational good and evil with this qualification).

مختار
تحسين وتقبيح
داعي
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari position denies human choice entirely, making praise and blame meaningless.
Correction: The Ash'ari view affirms that humans are compelled (majbūr) but in a 'mold of choice' (qālib mukhtār), meaning that while actions are ultimately from Allah, the servant experiences choice and is thus morally accountable.

Checkpoint
What is meant by 'the servant is compelled in a mold of choice'?
A) The servant has no choice at all.
B) The servant is forced to choose freely.
C) The servant's actions are ultimately determined by Allah, but the experience of choice and moral accountability apply.
D) The servant creates his own actions.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view does not eliminate moral responsibility; it redefines agency such that human acts are ultimately from Allah while humans are accountable due to the appearance of choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever subtly blame Allah's decree or your circumstances, forgetting that you genuinely experienced choice in that moment? How does acknowledging that your motive and power come from Allah change your repentance?
?
If you truly believed that every desire and ability to act is created by Allah, would you still feel the same remorse for your wrongdoings? Or would your guilt diminish? Examine your heart: do you use divine decree as a shield against accountability?
?
The lesson says the servant is 'compelled in a mold of choice.' Stand before Allah right now: will you, on the Day of Judgment, claim you were forced, or will you admit that you chose? What does your answer reveal about your current relationship with your own sins?

The Qadariyya's Second Objection: Praise, Blame, and Human Agency
Second Qadariyya objection and Ash'ari response denying entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qadariyya's argument that praise and blame require humans to be creators of their actions, and the Ash'ari response denying the logical entailment.

Core Idea
Praise and blame do not depend on creating actions, but on Divine justice and wisdom.

The Qadariyya raise a second objection: if humans do not create their own actions, how can they be praised or blamed? They argue that it is unjust to praise or blame someone for an act they did not truly originate. The Ash'ari response, as in the previous objection, is to deny the necessary connection between causal authorship and moral responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Identify the main claim: if the servant is not the creator (mukhtari') of his actions, praise and blame are invalid.
Notice the phrase 'mā taqarrara fī al-'urf' — 'what is established in custom'.
2
Understand the entailment
The Qadariyya assume a logical connection: no creation → no praise/blame. They rely on common intuition that one is not praised for another's act.
This is the mulāzama (entailment) that will be denied.
3
Examine the response
The footnote indicates the response: denial of the entailment (man' al-mulāzama). The Ash'ari position does not require human creation for moral responsibility.
The same answer as before — a recurring theme in these debates.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how the Ash'ari alternative grounds praise and blame in Divine wisdom and the human's acquisition (kasb) of actions, not in creation.
This preserves both Divine omnipotence and human accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجَّتِ القدريَّةُ أيضاً بأنَّ العبد لو لم يكنْ مُخترِعاً لأفعالِهِ لَمَا صح أن يُمدَحَ أو يُذَمَّ على فعلٍ مِنَ الأَفعالِ . وبيانُ الملازمةِ : ما تقرَّرَ في العرفِ مِنْ بطلان مدح الإنسانِ وذمّهِ بما يفعله غيرُهُ ، فإذا كانتِ الأفعالُ إنَّما صدرَتْ مِنَ الله تعالى فقط . صارَ مدح العبيد وذمهم إنَّما هو على فعلِ اللهِ جَلَّ وعلا ..
(۱) أي : قلنا : يجاب عما ذكر بجواب من معنى ما قبله ؛ وهو منع الملازمة ، وهذا جواب عن الطرفين . ( حامدي » ( ص ٣٥٦ ) .
The Qadariyya also argued that if the servant is not the creator (mukhtari') of his actions, it would not be valid to praise or blame him for any action. And the explanation of the entailment: what is established in custom is the invalidity of praising or blaming a person for what someone else does. So if actions only emanate from Allah the Exalted alone, then praising and blaming servants would be for the act of Allah, may He be glorified and exalted. (1) That is: We said: It is answered with an answer from the meaning of what preceded it, which is the denial of the entailment. And this is an answer regarding both sides. (al-Hāmidī, p. 356).
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection from the Qadariyya. They argue that if humans are not the creators (mukhtari'ūn) of their own acts, then praise and blame are invalid because it would mean praising or blaming someone for another's act (i.e., Allah's). The answer, provided in the footnote, states that the same response applies: the entailment is denied (man' al-mulāzama). In other words, the logical connection between 'not being the creator' and 'invalidity of praise/blame' is rejected. The Ash'ari position maintains that actions are signs of one's state, and reward/punishment are based on divine wisdom, not on human causal efficacy. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms

مخترع
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That being the 'creator' (mukhtari') of an action is a necessary condition for moral praise and blame.
Correction: In Ash'ari theology, moral responsibility is grounded in divine wisdom and the human's kasb (acquisition) of actions, not in creating them. The entailment from 'not creator' to 'no praise/blame' is denied.

Checkpoint
How do the Ahl al-Sunna respond to the Qadariyya objection that praise/blame require the servant to be the creator of his actions?
A) They affirm that the servant is the creator.
B) They deny the entailment between not being creator and invalidity of praise/blame.
C) They agree but say praise/blame are still possible by divine favor.
D) They say the objection only applies to the Qadariyya themselves.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response to the Qadariyya's second objection is to deny the logical entailment between not being the creator of actions and the invalidity of praise and blame.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own actions, do you truly believe that your ultimate accountability rests on God's wisdom and justice, or do you feel a deep need to see yourself as the independent author of your deeds? How does this affect your relationship with Allah?
?
If your praise or blame of yourself is ultimately based on what Allah creates in you, does that comfort you or disturb you? Can you sincerely accept that your moral worth is a gift from Allah rather than something you earn on your own?
?
Think of a time you felt proud of a good deed. According to this lesson, your act was created by Allah and merely acquired by you. How does this truth transform your pride into gratitude, and do you find that easy or difficult to practice?

Refuting the Qadariyya on Necessity of Creation for Praise
Sanusi denies the rational necessity behind the Qadariyya argument and criticizes reliance on custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Qadariyya argument that praise requires creation is rationally flawed and why reliance on custom is insufficient for rational judgments about God.

Core Idea
Praise does not rationally require the praised to be the creator of the action.

The Qadariyya claimed that if God alone creates actions, human praise and blame are meaningless. They based this on the custom that one is not praised for another's deeds. In this passage, Sanusi denies that there is any rational necessity connecting praise to creation, and he criticizes relying on mere customary usage, especially regarding God. This lesson examines that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Qadariyya argument
Read the first few lines where the Qadariyya present their argument that praise/blame require the servant to be the creator of the action.
Notice their reliance on customary practice.
2
Understand the claimed necessary connection
Identify the mulāzama (necessary connection) they assert between praise/blame and the agent being the creator.
The basis is 'urf (custom) – one is not praised for another's deed.
3
See Sanusi's answer
Sanusi denies that there is a rational necessity (lā mulāzama 'aqlan). This is the core refutation.
4
Critique of relying on custom
Sanusi argues that relying on mere customary convention for rational judgments, especially about God, is foolish and leads to error.
He even says it shows 'extreme stupidity'.
5
Consider the textual corruption
The Arabic contains unclear words like 'الأوهام' and 'سلَّمنا'. These may be scribal errors, but the argument remains clear.
Focus on the overall meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجَّتِ القدريَّةُ أيضاً بأنَّ العبد لو لم يكنْ مُخترِعاً لأفعالِهِ لَمَا صح أن يُمدَحَ أو يُذَمَّ على فعلٍ مِنَ الأَفعالِ
وبيانُ الملازمةِ : ما تقرَّرَ في العرفِ مِنْ بطلان مدح الإنسانِ وذمّهِ بما يفعله غيرُهُ ، فإذا كانتِ الأفعالُ إنَّما صدرَتْ مِنَ الله تعالى فقط .

والجواب على نهج ما سبق : أَنَّهُ لا ملازمة عقلاً بينَ المدح والذم

وبين كون سببهما مخترعاً للممدوح أو المذموم ، والاعتماد في الأحكام العقلية - سيَّما بالنسبة إليهِ جلَّ وعلا - على مُجرَّدِ عُرْفِ اصطلاحي لا ينضبط أمرُهُ . . مِنْ أدلَّ دليل على تناهي القوم في الغباوة ، وكونِ تملكتْ عقولهم ، ولم تتركها أنْ تنفُذُ لمراشدها ، على أنَّا لو لهم الاعتماد في هذهِ المسألةِ على العُرفِ . . لَمَا اقتضى أَنَّ سببَ المدح أو الدم لا بدَّ وأنْ يكونَ فعلاً للممدوح أو المذموم (١) ، كيف وقد تقرر المدحُ بالجمالِ وحشنِ الخلقِ ونحو ذلكَ ممَّا لا كسبَ للممدوح فيه أصلاً كما تقرَّرَ الدم بأضدادِهِ ، وتقرر مدح الجمادات وذمها كالثياب والأبنية ونحوها باعتبارِ ما اتَّصفَتْ بهِ مِنَ الأوصافِ مَعَ أنَّها لم تفعلها ولم تشعر بها أصلاً ؟!
وإذا كان معنى المدح : الثناء على الشيء بما اتَّصفَ بِهِ مِنَ المحاسن حالاً أو مآلاً ، والذمُّ ضدَّ ذلك .. حَسُنَ مِدْحُ مَنْ خَلَقَ اللهُ سبحانه لهم بمخض فضلِهِ وإحسانِهِ أمارات تدلُّ شرعاً على حصولِ الكمالاتِ الأخرويَّةِ لهم ، والمحاسن الجسمانية والروحانية؛ التي هي ما لا عين رأتْ ، ولا أذن سمعَتْ ، ولا خطر على قلب بشر ، كما يحسنُ ذمُّ مَنِ اتَّصف بأضدادها ، ولا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The Qadariyya also argued that if the servant is not the creator of his actions, it would not be sound for him to be praised or blamed for any action. And the demonstration of the necessary connection: what has been established in custom concerning the invalidity of praising or blaming a person for what another does. So if actions only emanate from God alone, then the praise and blame of servants would be for the act of God, may He be glorified and exalted. The answer is along the lines of what preceded: there is no necessary rational connection between praise and blame and between the cause of them being the creator of the praised or blamed. Reliance in rational judgments—especially regarding Him, may He be glorified—on mere customary usage that is not regulated is among the strongest evidence of the people's extreme stupidity and that their minds are possessed, not allowing them to reach their right guidance. Moreover, even if we were to rely on custom in this matter, it would not necessitate that the cause of praise or blame must be the act of the praised or blamed. For it is established that praise is for beauty and good character and the like, in which the praised has no acquisition at all, just as blame is established for their opposites. And praise of inanimate objects and blame of them, like clothes and buildings and the like, is established on account of the attributes they possess, even though they did not do them nor were they aware of them at all. And if the meaning of praise is commendation of a thing for the good qualities it possesses, currently or eventually, and blame is its opposite, then it is appropriate to praise those for whom God—glorified—creates, out of pure favor and beneficence, markers that indicate religiously the attainment of eschatological perfections for them, and bodily and spiritual beauties, which no eye has seen, ear heard, nor has occurred to the human heart. And similarly it is appropriate to blame those who possess opposites of them. And there is no power and no strength except through God.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage refutes a key Qadariyya argument: if humans do not create their own actions, praise and blame become meaningless. The Qadariyya claim a necessary connection (mulāzama) between an action being praiseworthy/blameworthy and the agent being its creator. They rely on custom (ʿurf) where one is not praised for another's deed. The answer denies this connection on rational grounds: there is no rational necessity linking praise to creation. Relying on mere convention for rational judgments—especially about God—is foolish. Even if custom were accepted, it does not require the praised to be the author; for example, praise for beauty or good character does not come from the person's acquisition, and inanimate objects are praised for their attributes without agency. The author then defines praise and blame: commendation for good qualities (present or future) or their opposite. Thus, it is appropriate for God to create in some people signs that lead to eschatological perfections and spiritual beauties, and they are praised for these divinely given attributes. Similarly, those with opposite attributes are blamed. The passage concludes with a pious formula.

Key Terms
قدريَّة
اختراع الأفعال
الملازمة
العرف الاصطلاحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Praise and blame necessarily require the agent to be the sole creator of the action.
Correction: Sanusi shows there is no rational necessity; custom does not prove it, and even custom does not imply it.

Checkpoint
What is the primary basis the Qadariyya use for their argument that praise requires creation?
A) Revelation
B) Rational necessity
C) Customary usage (ʿurf)
D) Philosophical principles

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya's reliance on custom to prove necessity is rationally invalid; there is no rational connection between praise and creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise someone for a good deed, do you instinctively assume they are the sole cause of that action? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what does it reveal about your need to redirect praise to Allah alone?
?
Are you ever tempted to rely on 'common sense' or societal norms to decide what is rational in religious matters? How does Sanusi’s critique of 'urf iṣṭilāḥī expose the danger of that habit in your own life?
?
Think of a time you blamed yourself harshly for a failure. In light of this lesson, how can you hold yourself accountable without falling into the Qadariyya error of assuming you create your own actions, and instead maintain humility before Allah’s sole creative power?

Praise and Blame Based on Attributes, Not Creation
Sanusi provides examples and a definition to show praise does not require creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how praise and blame can be based on divinely bestowed attributes, not only on self-produced actions, as shown by examples and definition.

Core Idea
Praise and blame are based on attributes, not necessarily on the agent's creation of the act.

Sanusi continues his refutation by providing examples where praise is given for qualities not acquired by the person (beauty, character) and even inanimate objects praised for their attributes. He then defines praise and blame as commendation for good qualities present or future, and applies this to the eschatological perfections God bestows upon some. This lesson explores these points.

Guided Path
1
Examine the examples of praise without creation
Read the examples: praise for beauty and good character, and praise for inanimate objects like clothes and buildings. Note that the praised did not create these attributes.
These examples disprove the Qadariyya requirement.
2
Understand the definition of praise and blame
Sanusi defines praise as commendation of a thing for its good qualities, present or future. Blame is its opposite. This definition does not involve creation.
3
Apply the definition to eschatological perfections
God creates in some people signs (amārāt) that indicate eschatological perfections. They are praised for these divinely given attributes, not for their own creation.
This directly refutes the Qadariyya.
4
Conclude with the appropriateness of praise and blame
Thus, it is appropriate to praise those with good attributes from God and blame those with opposite attributes. The argument of the Qadariyya is invalid.
The passage ends with a pious formula.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجَّتِ القدريَّةُ أيضاً بأنَّ العبد لو لم يكنْ مُخترِعاً لأفعالِهِ لَمَا صح أن يُمدَحَ أو يُذَمَّ على فعلٍ مِنَ الأَفعالِ
وبيانُ الملازمةِ : ما تقرَّرَ في العرفِ مِنْ بطلان مدح الإنسانِ وذمّهِ بما يفعله غيرُهُ ، فإذا كانتِ الأفعالُ إنَّما صدرَتْ مِنَ الله تعالى فقط .
صارَ مدح العبيد وذمهم إنَّما هو على فعلِ اللهِ جَلَّ وعلا
والجواب على نهج ما سبق : أَنَّهُ لا ملازمة عقلاً بينَ المدح والذم
الأوهام
سلَّمنا
و
وبين كون سببهما مخترعاً للممدوح أو المذموم ، والاعتماد في الأحكام العقلية - سيَّما بالنسبة إليهِ جلَّ وعلا - على مُجرَّدِ عُرْفِ اصطلاحي لا ينضبط أمرُهُ . . مِنْ أدلَّ دليل على تناهي القوم في الغباوة ، وكونِ تملكتْ عقولهم ، ولم تتركها أنْ تنفُذُ لمراشدها ، على أنَّا لو لهم الاعتماد في هذهِ المسألةِ على العُرفِ . . لَمَا اقتضى أَنَّ سببَ المدح أو الدم لا بدَّ وأنْ يكونَ فعلاً للممدوح أو المذموم (١) ، كيف وقد تقرر المدحُ بالجمالِ وحشنِ الخلقِ ونحو ذلكَ ممَّا لا كسبَ للممدوح فيه أصلاً كما تقرَّرَ الدم بأضدادِهِ ، وتقرر مدح الجمادات وذمها كالثياب والأبنية ونحوها باعتبارِ ما اتَّصفَتْ بهِ مِنَ الأوصافِ مَعَ أنَّها لم تفعلها ولم تشعر بها أصلاً ؟!
وإذا كان معنى المدح : الثناء على الشيء بما اتَّصفَ بِهِ مِنَ المحاسن حالاً أو مآلاً ، والذمُّ ضدَّ ذلك .. حَسُنَ مِدْحُ مَنْ خَلَقَ اللهُ سبحانه لهم بمخض فضلِهِ وإحسانِهِ أمارات تدلُّ شرعاً على حصولِ الكمالاتِ الأخرويَّةِ لهم ، والمحاسن الجسمانية والروحانية؛ التي هي ما لا عين رأتْ ، ولا أذن سمعَتْ ، ولا خطر على قلب بشر ، كما يحسنُ ذمُّ مَنِ اتَّصف بأضدادها ، ولا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The Qadariyya also argued that if the servant is not the creator of his actions, it would not be sound for him to be praised or blamed for any action. And the demonstration of the necessary connection: what has been established in custom concerning the invalidity of praising or blaming a person for what another does. So if actions only emanate from God alone, then the praise and blame of servants would be for the act of God, may He be glorified and exalted. The answer is along the lines of what preceded: there is no necessary rational connection between praise and blame and between the cause of them being the creator of the praised or blamed. Reliance in rational judgments—especially regarding Him, may He be glorified—on mere customary usage that is not regulated is among the strongest evidence of the people's extreme stupidity and that their minds are possessed, not allowing them to reach their right guidance. Moreover, even if we were to rely on custom in this matter, it would not necessitate that the cause of praise or blame must be the act of the praised or blamed. For it is established that praise is for beauty and good character and the like, in which the praised has no acquisition at all, just as blame is established for their opposites. And praise of inanimate objects and blame of them, like clothes and buildings and the like, is established on account of the attributes they possess, even though they did not do them nor were they aware of them at all. And if the meaning of praise is commendation of a thing for the good qualities it possesses, currently or eventually, and blame is its opposite, then it is appropriate to praise those for whom God—glorified—creates, out of pure favor and beneficence, markers that indicate religiously the attainment of eschatological perfections for them, and bodily and spiritual beauties, which no eye has seen, ear heard, nor has occurred to the human heart. And similarly it is appropriate to blame those who possess opposites of them. And there is no power and no strength except through God.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage refutes a key Qadariyya argument: if humans do not create their own actions, praise and blame become meaningless. The Qadariyya claim a necessary connection (mulāzama) between an action being praiseworthy/blameworthy and the agent being its creator. They rely on custom (ʿurf) where one is not praised for another's deed. The answer denies this connection on rational grounds: there is no rational necessity linking praise to creation. Relying on mere convention for rational judgments—especially about God—is foolish. Even if custom were accepted, it does not require the praised to be the author; for example, praise for beauty or good character does not come from the person's acquisition, and inanimate objects are praised for their attributes without agency. The author then defines praise and blame: commendation for good qualities (present or future) or their opposite. Thus, it is appropriate for God to create in some people signs that lead to eschatological perfections and spiritual beauties, and they are praised for these divinely given attributes. Similarly, those with opposite attributes are blamed. The passage concludes with a pious formula.

Key Terms

ذم
الثناء
أمارات
الكمالات الأخروية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Praise must be for an action the person themselves performed.
Correction: Sanusi shows praise can be for attributes not acquired or created by the person, including divinely bestowed perfections.

Checkpoint
Which example does Sanusi use to show that praise does not require the agent to have created the quality?
A) Praise for a good deed
B) Praise for beautiful clothes
C) Praise for courage in battle
D) Praise for knowledge acquired by study

Key Takeaway
Praise and blame are appropriate for divinely bestowed attributes, not only for actions the agent creates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a good quality you have—beauty, intelligence, or character. Have you ever taken pride in it as if you earned it yourself? After reading this passage, how does knowing that it is purely a gift from God change your feeling of pride? What specific step can you take today to turn that pride into gratitude?
?
Sanusi shows that inanimate objects are praised for attributes they did not choose. You are not an object—you have consciousness. Does that make it harder or easier to accept blame for faults you did not deliberately acquire? In the next hour, when you notice a flaw, will you blame yourself more or less? How can you use this lesson to avoid despair over your shortcomings?

The Mu'tazilite Objection: If God Creates Actions, Punishment Is Unjust
Mu'tazilite objection and its logical development

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that divine creation of all actions undermines the justice of punishment.

Core Idea
If God creates all actions, can He justly punish the disobedient?

The Mu'tazila raised a powerful objection to the Ash'ari view that God creates all human acts. They argued that if God creates both good and evil actions, then punishing the evil-doer is unjust because they had no choice. This passage presents that objection in vivid detail, imagining the disobedient confronting God on the Day of Judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the full force of the Mu'tazilite argument.
Notice the structure: premise, explanation of the correlation, and the imagined dialogue.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
The objection assumes that if God creates actions, then the disobedient have a valid proof against punishment. Understand this logical link.
The premise is that the slave does not create his actions.
3
Examine the imagined speech
The passage puts words into the mouths of the disobedient. Notice how they appeal to God's knowledge, will, and creation of their selves and actions.
This rhetorical device makes the objection more compelling.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Ask yourself: Is this objection valid? What would be the Ash'ari response? Prepare to engage with the answer in the next lesson.
This lesson sets up the problem; the answer follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا أيضاً : بأنَّ العبد لو لم يكن هو المخترع لأفعاله.. لكانَتْ للعصاة المعذَّبينَ حُجَّةٌ على الله تعالى في الآخرةِ . وبيانُ الملازمة : أنَّهم يقولونَ عندما يُؤمَرُ بهم إلى العذابِ : يا ربَّنا ؛ كيفَ تُعذِّبُنا على شيءٍ خلقتَهُ فينا وسبقَ بهِ علمُكَ وإرادتكَ منَّا ونحنُ لا قدرة لنا على إيجادِ شيءٍ مما أمرتنا بهِ ، أو إعدام شيء ممَّا نهيتنا عنه ، بل ذواتنا وأفعالنا كلُّها ملكُكَ ومخلوقكَ ، لا شريكَ لكَ في شيءٍ مِنْ ذلكَ ، فنحنُ ومَنْ أمَرْتَ بهم إلى النعيم سواء ، كلٌّ منَّا منقاد لحكمِكَ وقضائِكَ ، جارٍ على وَفْقِ علمك وقدرتك وإرادتِكَ ؟! فما بال أولئِكَ يتنعمونَ في الفردوس ومنازل النعيم ، ونحنُ نتردد فيما لا يُقدَرُ على وصفِهِ مِنَ العذاب الأليم في دركاتِ الجحيم ؟! والجواب : أنَّ مثارَ الغلط فيما توهّموهُ مِنَ الحَجَّةِ إِنَّمَا جَاءَهُم مِمَّا اعتقدوهُ أنَّ الثواب والعقابَ مُعلَّلانِ بالأعمال ، وقد سبق أنَّهما لا علة لهما ، وإِنَّما الأعمالُ أماراتٌ ، والثوابُ والعقاب بمخض اختياره تعالى فضلاً وعدلاً ، لا يُسأَلُ عما يفعل ونحنُ المسؤولونَ .
They also argued: that if the slave is not the creator of his actions, then the disobedient who are punished would have a proof against Allah on the Last Day. Explanation of the correlation: They say when they are led to punishment: O our Lord, how can You punish us for something that You created in us and Your knowledge and will preceded concerning us, while we have no ability to bring into existence anything You commanded us, nor to eliminate anything You forbade us? Rather, our selves and our actions are all Your possession and Your creation; there is no partner for You in any of that. So we and those whom You have commanded to bliss are equal; each of us is submissive to Your decree and judgment, proceeding in accordance with Your knowledge, power, and will. So why are those enjoying paradise and the abodes of bliss, while we are tossed about in indescribable painful punishment in the depths of hell? The answer: The source of error in what they imagined of the argument came to them from their belief that reward and punishment are caused by actions. And it has preceded that they have no cause; rather, actions are merely signs, and reward and punishment are by pure choice of Allah, exalted, as favor and justice. He is not asked about what He does, but we are asked.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Mu'tazila: if God creates all actions, then the disobedient can argue that they are not responsible and therefore punishment is unjust. The objection is stated in detail: they admit God's knowledge, will, and creation of their selves and actions, and question why they are punished while others are rewarded. The author answers that the error stems from assuming reward and punishment are caused (معللان) by actions. In Ash'ari theology, reward and punishment are not caused by actions; they are purely by God's choice (مخض اختياره), as favor to some and justice to others. Actions are only signs (أمارات) that indicate the state of the person. God is not questioned about His actions.

Key Terms
حجة
ملازمة
ثواب
عقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the Mu'tazilite objection in this passage?
A) God is unjust because He punishes without reason.
B) If God creates all actions, the disobedient have a valid proof against punishment.
C) The slave creates his own actions.
D) Reward and punishment are caused by actions.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that if God creates all human actions, then punishing the disobedient is unjust because they had no control.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every action you do is created by Allah, how would your attitude towards your own sins change? Would you still feel genuine remorse, or would you be tempted to blame Allah? Examine your heart: are there times you secretly resent Allah for your shortcomings because you know He decrees all things? Write a short prayer asking Allah to protect you from using His decree as an excuse.
?
The Mu'tazilite objection imagines the disobedient arguing with Allah on Judgment Day. In your own life, when you sin, do you catch yourself thinking 'Allah willed it, so it's not my fault'? How does this lesson challenge that excuse? Commit today to a specific change in how you view your personal accountability before Allah.
?
The disobedient in the objection claim they are 'equal to the believers in submission to Allah's decree.' But is your submission truly equal? In what area of your life are you still resisting Allah's decree instead of surrendering to it? Name one specific area and decide on an action that would reflect true submission.

The Ash'ari Answer: Reward and Punishment Are by God's Pure Choice
Ash'ari answer to the Mu'tazilite objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response that reward and punishment are not caused by actions but are based on God's pure choice.

Core Idea
Actions are signs, not causes, of reward and punishment.

The Ash'ari answer to the Mu'tazilite objection is concise but profound. Sanusi identifies the source of error: the belief that reward and punishment are caused by actions. Instead, he explains that actions are merely signs (أمارات), and God distributes reward and punishment by His pure choice as favor and justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the structure of the answer.
Notice that the answer directly addresses the assumption behind the objection.
2
Identify the error
Sanusi says the Mu'tazilite error is thinking that reward and punishment are caused (معللان) by actions.
This is the key point: actions do not cause reward or punishment.
3
Understand the alternative
Actions are only signs (أمارات) that indicate the state of the person, but the actual recompense is by God's pure choice (مخض اختياره).
Think of signs as markers that God uses, not as efficient causes.
4
Reflect on divine justice
The answer concludes that God is not questioned about what He does, but we are questioned. This upholds both divine sovereignty and human accountability.
This is a classic Ash'ari position: God's actions are not subject to external criteria of justice.

Source Text

Rather, our selves and our actions are all Your possession and Your creation; there is no partner for You in any of that. So we and those whom You have commanded to bliss are equal; each of us is submissive to Your decree and judgment, proceeding in accordance with Your knowledge, power, and will. So why are those enjoying paradise and the abodes of bliss, while we are tossed about in indescribable painful punishment in the depths of hell? The answer: The source of error in what they imagined of the argument came to them from their belief that reward and punishment are caused by actions. And it has preceded that they have no cause; rather, actions are merely signs, and reward and punishment are by pure choice of Allah, exalted, as favor and justice. He is not asked about what He does, but we are asked.

Refutation of Mu'tazilite View Leading to Determinism
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing their view entails compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite position on originated power logically entails compulsion, contrary to their intention.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's escape from determinism actually leads back to it.

The Mu'tazila attempted to preserve human free will by positing an originated power (قدرة حادثة) as the effective agent in voluntary actions. However, as this passage demonstrates, their own concessions about God's role in creating all causes undermine their position. By accepting that God creates the power, the desire, and the determination, the Mu'tazila unwittingly affirm that God compels the action. This lesson examines the logical refutation that exposes the internal inconsistency in their doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the argument from premise to conclusion.
The passage ends mid-sentence but the core logical point is complete.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Notice that the Mu'tazila claim the originated power is the effective agent in voluntary actions. This is what they think preserves free will.
3
Trace the logical consequence
Follow the conditional reasoning: if God creates all causes (power, desire, determination), then the action becomes necessary, meaning God compels it. See how this conclusion contradicts the Mu'tazila's aim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وممَّا يُبْطِلُ مذهبَ المعتزلة : أَنَّ ما فرُّوا منه هو لازم لهم ، وإنْ قالوا بأنَّ القدرة الحادثة هي المؤثرةُ في الأفعال الاختياريَّة ؛ وذلكَ لأنَّهم وافقوا على أنَّهُ جلَّ وعلا هو الخالق للقدرة الحادثة والداعي للفعلِ مِنَ الشهوة فيهِ ، وقوَّةِ تصميم العزمِ عليه ، ونحو أسباب الفعل ، وإذا كانَتْ أسباب وجودِ الفَعلِ كلُّها مِنَ اللهِ تعالى ، والفعلُ معَها واجبٌ لا يمكن تركُهُ .. فصار إذا هذا العبد الله تعالى هو الذي ألجأه إلى ذلكَ الفعل ؛ بأن خلق له جميع أسبابه وما يتوقَّفُ عليهِ ؛ بحيثُ لا يجدُ معَ تلك الأسباب انفكاكاً عن الفعل وهو سبحانه وتعالى مع ذلك عالم بما يفعلُ هذا العبدُ مِنْ طاعة أو
And among what invalidates the doctrine of the Mu'tazila: that what they fled from is necessary for them, even if they say that the originated power is the effective agent in voluntary actions. That is because they agree that He, exalted and majestic, is the creator of the originated power, and the motive for the action from the desire in it, and the strength of determination upon it, and similar causes of the action. And if all the causes of the existence of the action are from Allah, and the action with them is necessary and cannot be abandoned... then this slave's God is the one who compelled him to that action, by creating all its causes and what it depends on, such that with those causes he finds no separation from the action. And He, subhanahu wa ta'ala, with that, knows what this servant does of obedience or...
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite position by demonstrating that their own doctrine entails the very determinism they sought to avoid. The Mu'tazila claim that the originated power (القدرة الحادثة) is the effective agent in voluntary actions. But they agree that God creates that power, the desire, the determination, and all other causes. If all causes of an action are from God, then the action becomes necessary and inevitable. Thus, God has compelled the slave to the action. The passage begins but is cut off mid-sentence, but the point is clear: the Mu'tazilite position collapses into compulsion (الجبر).

Key Terms
مذهب المعتزلة
قدرة حادثة
أسباب الفعل
إلجاء
جبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believed that by positing an originated power as the effective agent, human free will is preserved and determinism avoided.
Correction: The author shows that since God creates that power and all other causes, the action becomes inevitable, resulting in compulsion (جبر), exactly what the Mu'tazila sought to escape.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the consequence of the Mu'tazilite view that God creates the power and all causes?
A) It preserves human free will.
B) It leads to determinism (compulsion).
C) It denies God's power.
D) It affirms that humans create their actions independently.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite position on originated power leads to compulsion because God creates all causes, making action inevitable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say that all your actions are by Allah's will, but do you truly live with the awareness that even your desire to obey or disobey is created by Him? How does this lesson expose the hidden pride in thinking your good deeds originate from yourself? Will you ask Allah to make you humble before His complete sovereignty?
?
When you sin, do you quickly blame your own weakness or the shaytan, secretly assuming you could have chosen otherwise? This passage shows that if God creates all causes, even your sin is encompassed by His decree. How does this truth both sober you and drive you to seek His mercy? Will you repent today with the realization that your only hope is His forgiveness, not your own power?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they could protect human responsibility by affirming originated power, but ended up with compulsion. What assumptions about your own free will might you be holding that are actually inconsistent with tawhid? Are you willing to submit your understanding of choice to Allah's absolute creation of all things? What concrete change in your reliance on Allah will you make after this lesson?
