Answering the Sinners' Objection
Sinners object to punishment; answer: works are not causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Sunni doctrine holds that reward and punishment are not caused by human actions but are purely by God's choice.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment are God's pure choice, not caused by works.

The Mu'tazila and others argue that if God creates everything, then sinners should not be punished. The Ash'ari response is that reward and punishment are not caused by actions; actions are merely indicators. This passage presents the sinners' imagined objection and the theological answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the sinners' objection
Read the first part of the passage where sinners argue why they should not be punished, given that God created all their actions and predestined them.
2
Identify the core error
Notice that the answer points out the error: they assumed reward and punishment are caused by works.
This is the key difference between Sunni and Mu'tazila theology.
3
Understand the correct doctrine
The passage states that works are mere indicators (amārāt) and that reward/punishment are by God's pure choice (bimaḥḍ ikhtiyārihi).
This upholds God's absolute sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيانُ الملازمة : أنَّهم يقولونَ عندما يُؤمَرُ بهم إلى العذابِ : يا ربَّنا ؛ كيفَ تُعذِّبُنا على شيءٍ خلقتَهُ فينا وسبقَ بهِ علمُكَ وإرادتكَ منَّا ونحنُ لا قدرة لنا على إيجادِ شيءٍ مما أمرتنا بهِ ، أو إعدام شيء ممَّا نهيتنا عنه ، بل ذواتنا وأفعالنا كلُّها ملكُكَ ومخلوقكَ ، لا شريكَ لكَ في شيءٍ مِنْ ذلكَ ، فنحنُ ومَنْ أمَرْتَ بهم إلى النعيم سواء ، كلٌّ منَّا منقاد لحكمِكَ وقضائِكَ ، جارٍ على وَفْقِ علمك وقدرتك وإرادتِكَ ؟! فما بال أولئِكَ يتنعمونَ في الفراديس ومنازل النعيم ، ونحنُ نتردد فيما لا يُقدَرُ على وصفِهِ مِنَ العذاب الأليم في دركاتِ الجحيم ؟! والجواب : أنَّ مثارَ الغلط فيما توهّموهُ مِنَ الحَجَّةِ إِنَّمَا جَاءَهُم مِمَّا اعتقدوهُ أنَّ الثواب والعقابَ مُعلَّلانِ بالأعمال ، وقد سبق أنَّهما لا علة لهما ، وإِنَّما الأعمالُ أماراتٌ ، والثوابُ والعقاب بمحض اختياره من الله تعالى فضلاً وعدلاً ، لا يُسأَلُ عما يفعلُ ونحنُ المسؤولونَ . وممَّا يُبْطِلُ مذهبَ المعتزلة : أَنَّ ما فرُّوا منه هو لازم لهم ، وإنْ قالوا بأنَّ القدرة الحادثة هي المؤثرةُ في الأفعال الاختياريَّة ؛ وذلكَ لأنَّهم وافقوا على أنَّهُ جلَّ وعلا هو الخالق للقدرة الحادثة والداعي للفعلِ مِنَ الشهوة فيهِ ، وقوَّةِ تصميم العزمِ عليه ، ونحو أسباب الفعل ، وإذا كانَتْ أسباب وجودِ الفَعلِ كلُّها مِنَ اللهِ تعالى ، والفعلُ معَها واجبٌ لا يمكن تركُهُ .. فصار هذا العبد الله تعالى هو الذي ألجأه إلى ذلكَ الفعل ؛ بأن خلق له جميع أسبابه وما يتوقَّفُ عليهِ ؛ بحيثُ لا يجدُ معَ تلك الأسباب انفكاكاً عن الفعل وهو سبحانه وتعالى مع ذلك عالم بما يفعلُ هذا العبدُ مِنْ طاعة أو معصية ، فكان للعاصي أنْ يحتج أيضاً على مذهبكم ـ لو صحتِ الحجَّةُ ـ بمثل ما يحتج به على مذهبنا بزعمكم ، فيقول : يا ربّ ؛ لي القدرة وأنت تعلمُ أنّي أعصي بها ؟! ولِمَ خلقْتَ ليَ الشهوة فيها ؟! بل ولِمَ خلقتني أصلاً إذْ علمْتَ أنِّي لستُ ممَّنْ يصلحُ لطاعتِكَ ؟! وإذْ خلقتني فلِمَ لم تُمثني صغيراً قبل أن أبلغ سنَّ التكليف ؟! وإذْ بلَّغتني سنّ التكليف فلِمَ لم تجعلني مجنوناً لا أميز الأرضَ مِنَ السماء ؟! فذلكَ أسهل عليَّ بكثيرٍ مِمَّا عَرَّضَتَني لَهُ مِنَ العذاب الذي لا يُطاقُ ، وإذ جعلتني عاقلاً فلِمَ كلَّفَتَني أصلاً ، وقد علمْتَ أنَّ التكليف لا يفيد في شيئاً ، بل هو من أعظم المصائبِ عليَّ ؟! وغير هذا ممَّا ينشأُ عن توهمات فاسدة . وإلى هذا المعنى أشرتُ بقولي : ( وأيضاً : يبطل بمسألة خلْقِ الداعي ... ) إلى آخره ؛ أي : يبطل تعليلُ الثواب والعقاب بالأعمال وإن قلنا جدلاً : إنَّ القدرة الحادثةَ تُؤثر في مقدورها .. بمسألة خلقِ الداعي ... إلى آخره، ومسألة العلم مع خلق الداعي والقدرة هي التي حَلَقَتْ لِحَى المعتزلة ، ولهذا قال بعضُ أذكيائهم : لولا مسألة العلم لتمَّتِ الدَّسَّةُ لنا .
And the explanation of the entailment: that they say, when they are ordered to the punishment: 'O our Lord, how can You punish us for something You created in us, and Your knowledge and will preceded it from us, while we have no power to bring about anything of what You commanded us, nor to eliminate anything of what You forbade us? Rather, our essences and all our actions are Your property and Your creation; You have no partner in any of that. So we and those whom You have ordered to bliss are equal; all of us are submissive to Your decree and judgment, running according to Your knowledge, power, and will. Why then do those ones enjoy in the paradises and abodes of bliss, while we are tossed about in what cannot be described of the painful punishment in the depths of hellfire?' And the answer: that the source of error in what they imagined as proof came to them only from their belief that reward and punishment are caused by works, whereas it has preceded that they have no cause; rather, works are indicators, and reward and punishment are by pure choice from God—bounty and justice—He is not asked about what He does, and we are the ones asked. And among what invalidates the doctrine of the Mu'tazila: that what they fled from is necessary for them, even if they say that the originated power is the effecter in voluntary actions; because they agreed that He, exalted and high, is the creator of the originated power, the motive for the act from the desire in it, the strength of resolve upon it, and similar causes of the act. When all causes of the existence of the act are from God, and the act along with them is necessary and cannot be abandoned, then this servant—God is the one who compelled him to that act by creating all its causes and what it depends on, such that he finds no dissociation from the act with those causes—and He, glorified and exalted, is with that knowing what this servant does of obedience or disobedience. Then it is possible for the sinner to also argue against your doctrine—if the argument were valid—with the like of what he argues against our doctrine, according to your claim, saying: 'O Lord, I have power and You know that I disobey with it? And why did You create desire in me for it? Rather, why did You create me at all, since You knew that I am not among those who are fit for Your obedience? And since You created me, why did You not let me die as a child before I reached the age of obligation? And since You made me reach the age of obligation, why did You not make me insane so that I do not distinguish earth from sky? For that would be much easier for me than what You exposed me to of unbearable punishment. And since You made me rational, why did You obligate me at all, while You knew that obligation does not benefit me in anything, rather it is one of the greatest calamities upon me?' And other than this, from what arises from corrupt fantasies. And to this meaning I referred by my saying: 'And also: it is invalidated by the issue of creating the motive...' to its end; that is, the attribution of reward and punishment to works is invalidated, even if we argue dialectically that the originated power effects its object, by the issue of creating the motive... etc., and the issue of knowledge along with creating the motive and power is that which shaved the beards of the Mu'tazila, and therefore some of their clever ones said: 'Were it not for the issue of knowledge, the trick would have succeeded for us.'
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position on reward and punishment. It begins with an imagined objection from the inhabitants of Hell: they argue that since God created everything in them—their actions, desires, and even their lack of power—they should not be punished, because they are no different from the inhabitants of Paradise in terms of submission to God's decree. The answer to this objection is that the error lies in thinking that reward and punishment are necessarily caused by actions. In Sunni doctrine, actions are merely signs or indicators (amārāt), not efficient causes. Reward and punishment are purely by God's choice: He bestows grace (faḍl) and justice (ʿadl) as He wills, and is not questioned. Then the author provides a further refutation of the Mu'tazila: even if one accepts their premise that the humanly originated power (qudra ḥāditha) is effective in causing actions, the same objection applies. Since God creates all the causes of an action—the power, the motive, the resolve—the act becomes necessary, and the doer has no real freedom to abstain. Therefore, the sinner can argue against the Mu'tazila in the same way they argue against the Ashʿarīs: asking why God gave them power knowing they would sin, why He created the desire, why He created them at all, etc. The author then references his own line: 'And also: it is invalidated by the issue of creating the motive...' meaning that the issue of 'khalaq al-dāʿī' (creation of the motive) combined with foreknowledge (ʿilm) is what truly refutes the Mu'tazila, because it shows that all factors are from God, so the sinner cannot be blamed. The passage ends with a anecdote: one of the clever Mu'tazila admitted that without the issue of foreknowledge, their argument might have succeeded.

الثواب والعقاب
الأعمال أمارات
محض اختياره

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reward and punishment are necessarily caused by human actions.
Correction: In Sunni theology, actions are merely indicators; reward and punishment are by God's pure choice, not caused by works.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the source of error in the sinners' objection against God's punishment?
A) They misunderstand that reward and punishment have no cause and are purely by God's choice.
B) They think that God is unjust for creating them with no power.
C) They believe that God's knowledge is limited.
D) They deny the existence of Paradise and Hell.

Key Takeaway
Reward and punishment are not caused by actions; they are pure gifts or punishments from God, who is not questioned about His justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own good deeds and sins, do you really believe they are just 'indicators' and not what earns you Paradise? How does that truth change your reliance on God's mercy right now, especially when you fall short?
?
If God is not questioned about His justice, what stops you from questioning His decrees in your own life—like why others suffer less or seem more blessed? Can you surrender that judgment to Him today?
?
This lesson says the condemned sinners' error was assuming fairness based on works. Do you hold that same assumption when you compare yourself to others? Will you instead thank God for His bounty and fear His justice, without demanding explanations?

Refuting the Mu'tazila by Their Own Premises
Refutation of Mu'tazila by their own premise of created power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how the Ash'ari argument turns the Mu'tazila's own doctrine of originated power against them, showing that the same objection applies even if power is effective.

Core Idea
Even granting Mu'tazila premises, God creates all causes, so the sinner can argue similarly.

The Ash'ari response deepens: even if we accept the Mu'tazila claim that originated power (qudra ḥāditha) truly causes actions, the problem remains because God creates all conditions—power, motive, resolve—making the act necessary. This allows the sinner to raise the same objection against the Mu'tazila, and the issue of creating the motive (khalaq al-dāʿī) combined with foreknowledge finally refutes them.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila believe that human power (qudra ḥāditha) is effective in causing voluntary actions, and that God is just in rewarding/punishing based on those actions.
2
See the Ash'ari rebuttal
The passage argues that even if power is effective, God creates all causes—the power, the motive, the resolve—so the act is necessitated, and the doer cannot avoid it.
Thus the same objection (why punish me?) applies.
3
Examine the hypothetical complaint
The sinner's hypothetical complaint to God under Mu'tazila premises: why give me power knowing I'd sin? Why create desire? Why create me at all?
This shows the Mu'tazila cannot escape the problem.
4
The final refutation: creating the motive and knowledge
The issue of 'creating the motive' (khalaq al-dāʿī) combined with God's foreknowledge leaves no room for human independence; even Mu'tazila admitted this was their weak point.
The phrase 'shaved the beards of the Mu'tazila' is a famous expression.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If humans have effective power (qudra ḥāditha), then they are justly rewarded/punished.
Correction: Even with effective power, since God creates all causes including the power and motive, the act is necessitated, and the same objection arises.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'issue that shaved the beards of the Mu'tazila'?
A) The issue of created power (qudra ḥāditha)
B) The issue of creating the motive (khalaq al-dāʿī) combined with foreknowledge
C) The issue of justice (ʿadl)
D) The issue of free will (ikhtiyār)

Key Takeaway
Even if humans possess effective power, God's creation of all causes makes the act necessary, so the Mu'tazila cannot escape the same objection they raise against the Ash'aris.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself mentally shifting blame to God for giving you the desire or opportunity? How does this passage expose that excuse as hollow, and what will you do the next time that thought arises?
?
If God knows every sin you will commit and creates all the causes for it, does that make you less responsible? Or does it drive you to plead with Him more earnestly to remove those causes from your path? What specific prayer will you make today?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they could build a system where human beings are truly responsible, but they ended up in the same trap. Where in your own life do you try to justify a sin by reasoning that God gave you a certain nature or circumstance? How can you truly own your choice while still acknowledging God's absolute sovereignty?

Apparent and Inner Perspectives on Human Acts
Sanusi distinguishes apparent and inner realities of human acts and defines kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human acts are considered both from an apparent perspective, where reward and punishment are meaningful, and from an inner perspective, where the act is not a true cause.

Core Idea
Human acts appear to be ours but in reality they are not causal except as legal indicators.

Sanusi delves into the apparent and inner dimensions of human actions. He explains that while humans appear to originate their acts—allowing for praise, blame, reward, and punishment—the inner reality shows that these acts are not true causes. This distinction is crucial for understanding the Ash'ari concept of kasb (acquisition).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to 'fṣaḥa riʿāyat al-amrayn'. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the two perspectives mentioned.
2
Identify the apparent perspective
Notice how Sanusi describes the slave as appearing to be an originator (mūjid), and that this is the sense in which some scholars interpret 'kasb'. This makes reward, punishment, praise, and blame appropriate.
The apparent perspective aligns with human custom and legal discourse.
3
Identify the inner perspective
Now look at the inner reality: the slave's action cannot be a true cause (sabab) for anything. However, the term 'cause' can be used in the sense of a legal indicator (amāra sharʿiyya).
This distinction prevents attributing any real causal power to humans.
4
Understand the reconciliation
Sanusi then shows how Qur'anic verses and hadith sometimes consider actions and sometimes negate their causal efficacy, reconciling them by noting the two perspectives: compulsion (jabr) in inner reality and choice (ikhtiyār) in the apparent customary sense.
His phrase 'fṣaḥa riʿāyat al-amrayn' (the consideration of both matters is sound) summarizes this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان العبدُ بحَسَبِ الظاهرِ كأنَّهُ مُوجِدٌ - وبهذا المعنى فسَّرَ بعضُهم معنى الكسب - فتعليقُ الثوابِ لهُ والعقاب على فعله حسنانِ شرعاً وعرفاً وعقلاً ، ولهذا يحسنُ أن يُمدَحَ ويُدم على تلك الأفعالِ . وأما إن نظرنا إلى الباطن وإلى حقيقة الأمر .. لم يصح جعلُ فعله سبباً لشيء ، اللهم إلا أنْ يُطلَقَ عليهِ لفظ السبب بمعنى الأمارة الشرعيَّةِ فصحيح ، وقد جاءَ القرآن والسنَّةُ بملاحظة الأفعال تارةً ؛ نحو : ادْخُلُوا الْجَنَّةَ بِمَا كُنتُمْ تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [النحل : ۳۲] ونحوه ، وتارةً بلغوه ؛ نحو : « لَا يَدْخُلُ الْجَنَّةَ أَحَدٌ بِعَمَلِهِ » (١) ، ولعله لملاحظة الأمرين : الجبرِ بحسَبِ ما في نفس الأمرِ ، والاختيار بحسَبِ الظاهرِ وعُرْفِ التخاطب ؛ وهو المرادُ مِنْ قولي : ( فصح رغي الأمرين ((۲) ويحتمل أن يكونَ ذلك الاختلافُ لملاحظة كونه أمارةً شرعيةً وملاحظة نفي الدلالة عنه عقلية (۳) ، والله تعالى أعلم واعلم : أنَّ لأهلِ السنَّةِ على المعتزلة إلزاماتٍ كثيرةً يطولُ تتبعها وفيما ذكرناه مِنْ ذلك كفايةٌ ، والله أعلم .
And since the slave is, according to the apparent, as if he is an originator—and in this sense some have interpreted the meaning of kasb (acquisition)—so attaching reward and punishment to his action is good legally, customarily, and rationally, and for this reason it is good that he be praised and blamed for those actions. But if we look to the inner and to the reality of the matter, it is not valid to make his action a cause for anything, unless the word 'cause' is applied to it in the sense of a legal indicator, then it is valid. And the Qur'an and Sunnah have come sometimes considering actions, such as: 'Enter Paradise for what you used to do' [al-Nahl 16:32] and similar, and sometimes [with the wording] like: 'None of you will enter Paradise by his deed'. And perhaps it is due to considering the two matters: compulsion according to what is in the real state of affairs, and choice according to the apparent and the custom of discourse; and that is what is meant by my statement: 'so the consideration of the two matters is sound'. And it is possible that this difference is due to considering it as a legal indicator and considering the negation of any rational indication from it. And Allah Most High knows best. And know that Ahl al-Sunnah have many necessary arguments against the Mu'tazila, whose following would be lengthy, and in what we have mentioned of that there is sufficiency. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of the two aspects (apparent and inner) and the concept of kasb (acquisition). The author builds on the previous point: since the slave appears to be the originator (mūjid) of his action—and some scholars interpret 'acquisition' (kasb) precisely as this apparent origination—it is good (ḥasan) legally, customarily, and rationally to attach reward and punishment to the slave's action. Hence, praise and blame are fitting. However, when one examines the inner reality (al-bāṭin) and the true state of affairs (ḥaqīqat al-amr), the slave's action cannot be considered a cause (sabab) for anything in a real sense. The only exception is if 'cause' is used in the sense of a legal indicator (amāra sharʿiyya) that signals reward or punishment, which is valid. The author then notes that both the Qur'an and Sunnah sometimes consider actions as factors (e.g., 'Enter Paradise for what you used to do') and sometimes negate their causal efficacy (e.g., 'None of you will enter Paradise by his deed'). This apparent discrepancy is reconciled by considering the two perspectives: compulsion (jabr) in the real, inner sense, and choice (ikhtiyār) in the apparent, customary sense of discourse. The author's own phrase 'fṣaḥa riʿāyat al-amrayn' (the consideration of both matters is sound) encapsulates this. Alternatively, the difference might be because sometimes the action is seen as a legal indicator (amāra sharʿiyya) of reward or punishment, and sometimes the negation of any rational (ʿaqliyya) indication from it is intended. The passage concludes by noting that Ahl al-Sunnah have many necessary arguments (ilzāmāt) against the Mu'tazila, but what has been mentioned is sufficient.

Key Terms
كسب
ظاهر
باطن
سبب
أمارة شرعية
جبر
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think humans create their own actions, implying full causal independence from God.
Correction: The passage clarifies that humans only appear to originate their actions; in reality, God is the sole creator, and human acts are not true causes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what sense of 'cause' (sabab) can be applied to human actions from the inner perspective?
A) In the real, ontological sense.
B) Only as a legal indicator (amāra sharʿiyya).
C) As a true cause of reward in the Hereafter.
D) The term 'cause' cannot be applied at all.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view reconciles human responsibility with divine omnipotence by viewing actions from two perspectives: apparent (human choice) and inner (God's creation), with kasb as the apparent origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of a good deed you did today, do you instinctively feel pride as if you originated it? How would your heart change if you truly believed that deed was created by Allah and only attributed to you as an acquisition? Take a moment to thank Allah for that deed and ask Him to accept it.
?
The Quran says 'Enter Paradise for what you used to do' and also 'None of you will enter Paradise by his deed'. Have you ever struggled between feeling entitled to reward and feeling helpless? How does the two-perspective view free you from both arrogance and despair? Commit to recite one of these verses tonight and reflect on which perspective you need most.
?
Is there an area of your life where you rely on your own effort as if you are the cause of success? Write one such area now. Then, in light of this lesson, ask yourself: Can you still work hard while knowing that only Allah creates the outcome? Practice that surrender today.

Reconciling Apparent Contradictions in Revelation
Sanusi offers an alternative reconciliation for scriptural differences and concludes with a remark on Ahl al-Sunnah's arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to reconcile Quranic verses and hadith that seem to attribute reward to human deeds with those that deny causal efficacy to human actions.

Core Idea
Quranic statements about human deeds can be harmonized by considering them as legal indicators rather than true causes.

Sanusi now addresses a potential difficulty: how to understand scriptural texts that sometimes attribute reward to actions and sometimes deny salvation by deeds. He offers a second reconciliation, showing that these texts do not contradict but reflect different considerations—either the two perspectives (apparent vs. inner) or the role of actions as legal indicators versus rational indicators.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read from 'Wa yumkinu' to the end of the passage. Then read the translation.
2
Identify the two possible explanations
The author suggests that the difference in scriptural statements may be due to either (a) considering the two matters (jabr and ikhtiyar, as explained before) or (b) considering actions as legal indicators (amāra sharʿiyya) versus having no rational causal indication.
Both explanations are valid and consistent with Ash'ari theology.
3
Reflect on the concluding remark
Sanusi concludes that Ahl al-Sunnah have many arguments against the Mu'tazila, and what has been mentioned is sufficient. This underscores the strength of the Ash'ari position on human acts.
This remark ties the discussion back to broader sectarian debates.

The Apparent Perspective and the Basis of Reward
Sanusi distinguishes the apparent and inner perspectives on human agency and introduces the legal indicator concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari concept of kasb and why reward and punishment are attached to human actions from the apparent perspective, despite God's ultimate causality.

Core Idea
Humans appear to create their actions, but true causality belongs to God.

Sanusi begins by examining human actions from two angles: the apparent (ẓāhir) and the inner (bāṭin). He first explains that from the apparent perspective, the servant seems to be the agent, and this justifies praise and blame. This lesson explores that apparent perspective and the meaning of 'cause' as a legal indicator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two perspectives
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Observe the shift from ẓāhir to bāṭin.
2
Identify the definition of kasb
Some interpret kasb as the servant appearing to create his action. Note this.
3
Understand why reward/punishment are good
From the apparent perspective, it is good by law, custom, and reason to attach reward/punishment.
4
Notice the limitation of 'cause'
In inner reality, the action is not a cause except as a legal indicator.
This preserves God's sole causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان العبدُ بحَسَبِ الظاهرِ كأنَّهُ مُوجِدٌ - وبهذا المعنى فسَّرَ بعضُهم معنى الكسب - فتعليقُ الثوابِ لهُ والعقاب على فعله حسنانِ شرعاً وعرفاً وعقلاً ، ولهذا يحسنُ أن يُمدَحَ ويُدم على تلك الأفعالِ . وأما إن نظرنا إلى الباطن وإلى حقيقة الأمر .. لم يصح جعلُ فعله سبباً لشيء ، اللهم إلا أنْ يُطلَقَ عليهِ لفظ السبب بمعنى الأمارة الشرعيَّةِ فصحيح ، وقد جاءَ القرآن والسنَّةُ بملاحظة الأفعال تارةً ؛ نحو : ادْخُلُوا الْجَنَّةَ بِمَا كُنتُمْ تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [النحل : ۳۲] ونحوه ، وتارةً بلغوه ؛ نحو : « لَا يَدْخُلُ الْجَنَّةَ أَحَدٌ بِعَمَلِهِ » ، ولعله لملاحظة الأمرين : الجبرِ بحسَبِ ما في نفس الأمرِ ، والاختيار بحسَبِ الظاهرِ وعُرْفِ التخاطب ؛ وهو المرادُ مِنْ قولي : ( فصح رغي الأمرين ) ويحتمل أن يكونَ ذلك الاختلافُ لملاحظة كونه أمارةً شرعيةً وملاحظة نفي الدلالة عنه عقلية ، والله تعالى أعلم واعلم : أنَّ لأهلِ السنَّةِ على المعتزلة إلزاماتٍ كثيرةً يطولُ تتبعها وفيما ذكرناه مِنْ ذلك كفايةٌ ، والله أعلم .
And when the servant is, according to the apparent, as if he is the one who creates (mūjid) — and by this meaning some interpreted the meaning of kasb (acquisition) — then the attaching of reward and punishment to his action is good according to law, custom, and reason. And for this reason it is good that he be praised and blamed for those actions. But if we look to the inner (bāṭin) and to the reality of the matter, it is not valid to make his action a cause for anything, except if the word 'cause' is applied to it in the sense of a legal indicator (amāra shar‘iyya), then it is correct. And the Qur’an and Sunnah have come, sometimes considering actions: like 'Enter Paradise because of what you used to do' [al-Nahl 16:32] and similar, and sometimes negating it: like 'None will enter Paradise by his deed'. And perhaps this is due to observing two matters: predestination (jabr) according to the real nature of the affair, and choice (ikhtiyār) according to the apparent and the custom of discourse. And this is what is meant by my statement: 'so both matters are valid.' And it is possible that this difference is due to observing that it is a legal indicator and observing the negation of rational indication from it. And Allah knows best. And know: the Ahlu al-Sunnah have many arguments against the Mu‘tazila, whose tracing would be lengthy, and what we have mentioned of that suffices. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb (acquisition) and the basis for assigning reward and punishment to human actions. The author first states that from an apparent (ẓāhir) perspective, the servant appears to be the creator (mūjid) of his action, and this is the interpretation some give to kasb. Consequently, it is good (ḥasan) by law, custom, and reason to attach reward and punishment to the servant's action, and thus praise and blame are appropriate. However, when considering the inner reality (bāṭin) and the true nature of things, the servant's action cannot be a cause in itself; it is only a cause in the sense of a legal indicator (amāra shar‘iyya), meaning that God has made it a sign that triggers reward or punishment, not that the action independently produces the effect. The author then cites Quranic verses and hadith that sometimes attribute reward to deeds (e.g., 'Enter Paradise because of what you used to do') and sometimes negate it (e.g., 'None will enter Paradise by his deed'). This apparent contradiction is reconciled by noting two perspectives: real predestination (jabr) according to the divine decree, and apparent choice (ikhtiyār) according to human custom and discourse. Therefore, both statements are valid (fṣaḥa rghay al-amrayn). Alternatively, the difference could be due to considering the action as a legal indicator versus considering the negation of rational indication. The passage concludes that the Ahlu al-Sunnah have many compelling arguments against the Mu'tazila, and what has been mentioned is sufficient.

Key Terms
كسب (kasb)
ظاهر (ẓāhir)
باطن (bāṭin)
أمارة شرعية (amāra shar‘iyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates all actions, then humans are not truly responsible, so reward and punishment are unjust.
Correction: The Ash'ari position holds that from the apparent perspective, the servant is seen as the agent, and God attaches reward and punishment as legal indicators, which is just and good by law, custom, and reason.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it valid to praise or blame the servant for his actions from the apparent perspective?
A) Because the servant is the true creator.
B) Because the servant appears to be the creator and reward/punishment are attached as legal indicators.
C) Because actions have intrinsic moral value.
D) Because God is not involved.

Key Takeaway
From the apparent perspective, human actions are the basis for reward and punishment as legal indicators, not as true causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are praised or blamed for an action, do you truly believe deep down that God is the only creator of that action, or do you feel a sense of personal ownership and causality that contradicts this lesson? What would change in your heart if you fully internalized that your deeds are merely indicators by God's decree?
?
Think of an action you recently did for which you felt proud. Can you honestly say you see it not as your own creation but as a legal indicator from God? How does this shift affect your sense of entitlement to reward or praise? Will you examine your next deed this way?
?
The passage says it is 'good' by law, custom, and reason to attach reward and punishment to the apparent perspective. Yet the inner reality denies your action as a cause. Are you living in a double standard—acting as if you are the cause while knowing God is? Which perspective will you let govern your gratitude and repentance today?

Reconciling Divine Decree and Human Accountability
Sanusi reconciles contradictory Quranic verses by appealing to two perspectives and the concept of legal indicators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi reconciles Quranic verses that seem to affirm and negate the role of deeds in entering Paradise.

Core Idea
Deeds are both affirmed and negated: affirmed as legal indicators, negated as independent causes.

The Quran and Sunnah contain verses that sometimes attribute entering Paradise to deeds and sometimes negate it. Sanusi explains that this apparent contradiction is resolved by considering two perspectives: ultimate divine decree (jabr) and apparent human choice (ikhtiyār). This lesson examines that reconciliation and the role of legal indicators.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of Quranic verses
One type affirms deeds, the other negates. Find the examples given.
2
Understand the first reconciliation
Sanusi says it may be due to observing jabr and ikhtiyār. Explain this in your own words.
3
Understand the second reconciliation
Alternatively, it may be due to considering the action as a legal indicator vs. negating rational indication.
4
Note the conclusion about Mu'tazila
Sanusi states that the arguments of Ahl al-Sunnah against Mu'tazila are sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان العبدُ بحَسَبِ الظاهرِ كأنَّهُ مُوجِدٌ - وبهذا المعنى فسَّرَ بعضُهم معنى الكسب - فتعليقُ الثوابِ لهُ والعقاب على فعله حسنانِ شرعاً وعرفاً وعقلاً ، ولهذا يحسنُ أن يُمدَحَ ويُدم على تلك الأفعالِ . وأما إن نظرنا إلى الباطن وإلى حقيقة الأمر .. لم يصح جعلُ فعله سبباً لشيء ، اللهم إلا أنْ يُطلَقَ عليهِ لفظ السبب بمعنى الأمارة الشرعيَّةِ فصحيح ، وقد جاءَ القرآن والسنَّةُ بملاحظة الأفعال تارةً ؛ نحو : ادْخُلُوا الْجَنَّةَ بِمَا كُنتُمْ تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [النحل : ۳۲] ونحوه ، وتارةً بلغوه ؛ نحو : « لَا يَدْخُلُ الْجَنَّةَ أَحَدٌ بِعَمَلِهِ » ، ولعله لملاحظة الأمرين : الجبرِ بحسَبِ ما في نفس الأمرِ ، والاختيار بحسَبِ الظاهرِ وعُرْفِ التخاطب ؛ وهو المرادُ مِنْ قولي : ( فصح رغي الأمرين ) ويحتمل أن يكونَ ذلك الاختلافُ لملاحظة كونه أمارةً شرعيةً وملاحظة نفي الدلالة عنه عقلية ، والله تعالى أعلم واعلم : أنَّ لأهلِ السنَّةِ على المعتزلة إلزاماتٍ كثيرةً يطولُ تتبعها وفيما ذكرناه مِنْ ذلك كفايةٌ ، والله أعلم .
And when the servant is, according to the apparent, as if he is the one who creates (mūjid) — and by this meaning some interpreted the meaning of kasb (acquisition) — then the attaching of reward and punishment to his action is good according to law, custom, and reason. And for this reason it is good that he be praised and blamed for those actions. But if we look to the inner (bāṭin) and to the reality of the matter, it is not valid to make his action a cause for anything, except if the word 'cause' is applied to it in the sense of a legal indicator (amāra shar‘iyya), then it is correct. And the Qur’an and Sunnah have come, sometimes considering actions: like 'Enter Paradise because of what you used to do' [al-Nahl 16:32] and similar, and sometimes negating it: like 'None will enter Paradise by his deed'. And perhaps this is due to observing two matters: predestination (jabr) according to the real nature of the affair, and choice (ikhtiyār) according to the apparent and the custom of discourse. And this is what is meant by my statement: 'so both matters are valid.' And it is possible that this difference is due to observing that it is a legal indicator and observing the negation of rational indication from it. And Allah knows best. And know: the Ahlu al-Sunnah have many arguments against the Mu‘tazila, whose tracing would be lengthy, and what we have mentioned of that suffices. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb (acquisition) and the basis for assigning reward and punishment to human actions. The author first states that from an apparent (ẓāhir) perspective, the servant appears to be the creator (mūjid) of his action, and this is the interpretation some give to kasb. Consequently, it is good (ḥasan) by law, custom, and reason to attach reward and punishment to the servant's action, and thus praise and blame are appropriate. However, when considering the inner reality (bāṭin) and the true nature of things, the servant's action cannot be a cause in itself; it is only a cause in the sense of a legal indicator (amāra shar‘iyya), meaning that God has made it a sign that triggers reward or punishment, not that the action independently produces the effect. The author then cites Quranic verses and hadith that sometimes attribute reward to deeds (e.g., 'Enter Paradise because of what you used to do') and sometimes negate it (e.g., 'None will enter Paradise by his deed'). This apparent contradiction is reconciled by noting two perspectives: real predestination (jabr) according to the divine decree, and apparent choice (ikhtiyār) according to human custom and discourse. Therefore, both statements are valid (fṣaḥa rghay al-amrayn). Alternatively, the difference could be due to considering the action as a legal indicator versus considering the negation of rational indication. The passage concludes that the Ahlu al-Sunnah have many compelling arguments against the Mu'tazila, and what has been mentioned is sufficient.

Key Terms
جبر (jabr)
اختيار (ikhtiyār)
أمارة شرعية (amāra shar‘iyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran contradicts itself by saying deeds matter and then saying they do not.
Correction: The verses are reconciled by considering different perspectives: ultimate divine causality vs. apparent human agency, or legal indicator vs. rational cause.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, how are Quranic verses that affirm and negate the role of deeds in entering Paradise reconciled?
A) By abrogation.
B) By considering two perspectives: divine predestination and apparent choice.
C) By interpreting 'deed' differently.
D) By rejecting the negating verses.

Key Takeaway
Quranic verses that affirm and negate the role of deeds are reconciled by viewing them from the perspectives of divine decree and human choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you instinctively blame your own choice or feel that it was destined? The lesson teaches that both perspectives are true in different ways. Which perspective do you actually live by in your moments of failure, and what does that reveal about your heart’s reliance on Allah? This week, when you stumble, deliberately pause to affirm both jabr and ikhtiyar—how does that change your repentance?
?
The verses that negate deeds threaten any pride in your worship. Do you secretly believe your good deeds put Allah in your debt, even after hearing this lesson? Examine your prayer and charity: are they performed to 'earn' Paradise, or as a response to mercy? Commit to one act of worship today with the pure intention that it is purely by Allah’s grace, not your merit.
?
Reconciling these verses requires accepting a mystery. Are you comfortable with not fully grasping how decree and choice coexist, or does this tension unsettle you? The lesson says 'both matters are valid.' Can you truly trust Allah’s wisdom enough to hold both truths without reducing one? Right now, identify a specific area of your life where you struggle to accept this mystery, and surrender it to Allah with full reliance.

Refutation of the Doctrine of Tawallud
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazili doctrine of tawallud (generated acts) by showing its logical absurdities and noting scholarly consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school rejects the Mu'tazili doctrine of tawallud (generated acts) and the rational arguments used to refute it.

Core Idea
Generated acts (tawallud) are impossible because a created power cannot produce effects outside its own locus.

Sanusi presents a concise refutation of the Mu'tazili doctrine of tawallud, which claims that actions can generate other actions through indirect causality. Building on the previously established impossibility of a created power affecting its own locus, he argues that such a power cannot affect something else through its effect. This lesson unpacks the logical absurdities that follow from accepting tawallud.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: premise, examples, labeling, absurdities, and additional consensus.
The passage is dense with key terms; focus on understanding the flow.
2
Understand the key premise
The argument rests on the prior proof that a created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) cannot affect its own locus. If that is impossible, then affecting another locus through its object is also impossible.
This premise was established earlier; here it is applied to tawallud.
3
Examine the examples
Sanusi provides examples: throwing a stone and striking with a sword. These are typical cases where tawallud is claimed: the movement of the hand generates the stone's motion or the sword's cut.
4
Identify the three absurdities
He lists three absurdities that follow from tawallud: (1) an effect between two causes, (2) an act without an agent, (3) an agent without will or knowledge of the object. Understand why each is impossible.
These are elaborated in longer works (al-muṭawwalāt).
5
Consider the additional consensus argument
The majority of scholars agree that satiety and thirst are not generated from eating and drinking. This consensus further undermines the doctrine of tawallud.
This shows that even in natural processes, generation is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصلُ في إبطال القول بالتولد وإذا عرفت استحالة تأثير القدرة الحادثة في محلها .. بطل لذلك أيضاً تأثيرها بواسطة مقدورها في غير محلها؛ كرمي الحجرِ والضرب بالسيفِ ونحو ذلكَ ممَّا يُوجَدُ عادةً بواسطة حركة اليد مثلاً ، وهو المسمَّى بالتولدِ عند القدرية مجوس هذهِ الأُمَّةِ ، معَ ما فيهِ على مذهبهم مِنْ وجودِ أَثرِ بينَ مُؤثرينِ ووجودِ فعلٍ مِنْ غيرِ فاعل ، أو فاعل مِنْ غيرِ إرادة ولا علم بالمفعول، ونحو ذلكَ مِنَ الاستحالاتِ المذكورة في المطولات . واتَّفق الأكثرُ على عدم تولدِ الشبع والرَّيِّ ونحوهما عنِ الأكل والشرب وشبههما ، وذلك مما ينقصُ أيضاً على القائلين بالتولد ، وبالله التوفيق
Chapter on nullifying the doctrine of tawallud (generation of acts). And when you know the impossibility of the effect of the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) in its own locus, then therefore its effect through its object of power (maqdūr) in something other than its locus is also invalidated; like throwing a stone and striking with a sword and similar things that are usually found by means of the movement of the hand, for example. And this is what is called tawallud by the Qadariyya, the Magians of this Ummah, along with what is in it according to their school of the existence of an effect between two causes, and the existence of an act without an agent, or an agent without will or knowledge of the object, and similar impossibilities mentioned in the lengthy works. And the majority agreed on the non-generation of satiety, thirst, and similar from eating, drinking, and their likes. And this also counts against those who affirm tawallud. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili doctrine of tawallud (generation of acts). The author begins by stating that if one understands the impossibility of a created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) producing an effect in its own locus (i.e., the limb where the power resides cannot create its own movement because that would require the power to act upon itself, which is impossible), then it follows that such power cannot produce an effect through its object of power in a different locus. Examples include the generation of injury from throwing a stone or striking with a sword, which are usually observed to occur via the movement of the hand. This doctrine of tawallud is attributed to the Qadariyya (the Qadari sect), whom the author calls the Magians of this Ummah, because of their belief in autonomous causality. The author then points out several absurdities that follow from tawallud: (1) the existence of an effect between two causes (a single effect having two independent causes), (2) the existence of an act without an agent (the stone's motion being an act without a direct agent), (3) the existence of an agent without will or knowledge of the outcome (the hand does not intend the injury from the stone in a generative sense). These are impossibilities detailed in longer works. Furthermore, the majority of scholars agree that satiety, thirst, and similar states cannot be generated by eating and drinking; they are directly created by God. This consensus also argues against those who affirm tawallud.

Key Terms
تولد (tawallud)
قدرية (Qadariyya)
قدرة حادثة (qudra ḥāditha)
مقدور (maqdūr)
أثر بين مؤثرين (athar bayna mu’aththirayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Created powers can independently generate effects through secondary causality (tawallud).
Correction: Ash'ari theology holds that all effects are directly created by God; no created power can produce an effect outside its own locus, and apparent causal chains are ingrained habits (ʿāda).

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT an absurdity that the author claims follows from the doctrine of tawallud?
A) An effect between two causes.
B) An act without an agent.
C) An agent without will or knowledge.
D) The impossibility of satiety from eating.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of tawallud (generated acts) is invalid because it leads to logical absurdities such as effect between two causes, act without agent, and agent without will, and it contradicts the consensus that states like satiety and thirst are not generated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your actions, like throwing a stone, independently cause the stone's movement? Reflect on a recent situation where you attributed a result solely to your own effort. How does this passage challenge that assumption? Will you now consciously remind yourself that Allah is the direct Creator of all effects?
?
The Qadariyya are called the Magians of this Ummah for their belief in autonomous secondary causality. In what subtle ways might you be acting like a 'Magian' by attributing outcomes to your own power rather than to Allah? What specific thought pattern will you change today?
?
The passage lists the absurdity of an act without an agent. When you take credit for a success, are you implicitly committing the same error? How can you cultivate a constant awareness that every effect is directly from Allah, starting from your next action?

Refuting the Necessity of the Optimal for Servants
Sanusi refutes the view that God is obligated to do what is best for servants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that God is not obligated to do what is best for His servants, and how he uses reductio ad absurdum to refute the Mu'tazilite view.

Core Idea
God's freedom is not limited by any obligation to do what is best for creation.

Sanusi presents a series of logical arguments against the Mu'tazilite claim that God must always choose what is optimal for His servants. He shows that if such an obligation existed, it would lead to absurd consequences that contradict observable reality. This passage demonstrates the Ash'ari position that God's actions are not subject to external obligations, but are based on His wisdom and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Get a sense of the argument flow.
2
Identify the first argument
Notice that Sanusi argues: if well-being were obligatory, then God would not impose religious obligations (taklif), because that exposes servants to sin.
This is a reductio: the premise leads to a conclusion contrary to reality.
3
Consider the objection and response
Read the objection that taklif is for reward, and Sanusi's response that God can grant reward without it. Why does this strengthen the argument?
The response shows that the purpose of taklif is not merely reward.
4
Examine the second and third arguments
Next, Sanusi argues that if the optimal were obligatory, God would not create the poor disbeliever (since non-existence is better) and would create all directly in Paradise.
Both examples show that the 'optimal' obligation would prevent normal creation.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The summary states that no trial would exist. Recognize that since trials exist, the premise that optimal is obligatory is false.
This confirms the Ash'ari position: God is free and not externally obligated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : لو وجبَ عليهِ صلاح العبدِ لَمَا كَلَّفَهُ ؛ لما فيهِ مِنْ تعريضِهِ للمعصية . فإن قيل : إنَّما كَلَّفَهُ ليثيبه قلنا : هو قادرٌ أن يعطيه ذلكَ مِنْ غيرِ عمل ولا تكليف . ولو وجبَ عليهِ الأصلحُ لَمَا خلق الكافر الفقيرَ ؛ لأنَّ الأصلح لهُ ألا يخلقَهُ ؛ حتى لا يكونَ مُعذَّباً في الدنيا والآخرةِ . وأيضاً : الأصلحُ للعبادِ أن يخلقهم في الجنَّةِ ، فلو وجب عليهِ لَمَا خلقهم في الدنيا . وبالجملة : لو وجبَ عليهِ الأصلحُ لَمَا وُجِدَتْ محنةٌ دنيويَّةٌ ولا أخروية
And also: if the well-being (ṣalāḥ) of the servant were obligatory upon Him, He would not have burdened him with obligation (taklīf), because that would expose him to disobedience. If it is said: He only burdened him in order to reward him; we say: He is able to give him that without work and without obligation. And if the optimal (al-aṣlaḥ) were obligatory upon Him, He would not have created the poor disbeliever, because the optimal for him is that He not create him, so that he would not be tormented in this world and the Hereafter. And also: the optimal for the servants is that He create them in Paradise, so if it were obligatory upon Him, He would not have created them in this world. In sum: if the optimal were obligatory upon Him, no worldly or otherworldly trial would have existed.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage presents a series of reductio ad absurdum arguments against the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God is obliged to do what is best (al-aṣlaḥ) for His servants. The author argues: If God were obligated to ensure the servant's well-being, He would not have imposed religious obligations (taklīf) because obligations expose the servant to the possibility of sin and punishment, which contradicts the servant's well-being. To the objection that obligation is for the sake of reward, the author responds that God can grant reward without any work or obligation, so obligation is unnecessary if the goal is well-being. Next, if the optimal were obligatory, God would not have created a poor disbeliever, because non-existence is better for him than existence leading to eternal torment. Similarly, the optimal for all servants would be to be created directly in Paradise, so God would not have created them in this world of trials. The conclusion is that if the optimal were obligatory, no trial or test would exist at all, which is clearly false given the existence of worldly and otherworldly trials. This proves that God is not bound by any external obligation.

Key Terms
صلاح
أصلح
تكليف
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that God's justice requires Him to always do what is best for the servant.
Correction: God is free and just without being obliged to do what is best; He acts according to His wisdom.

Checkpoint
What is one argument given against the necessity of the optimal (al-aṣlaḥ) for servants?
A) If it were obligatory, God would have created everyone in Paradise immediately
B) God is unable to create the optimal
C) The optimal is unknown to God
D) Servants do not desire the optimal

Key Takeaway
God is not obligated to do what is best for His servants, as the existence of trials proves that He acts according to His wisdom, not external necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or see others suffering, do you ever feel that God owes you an easier path? How does Sanusi's argument—that God is not obliged to give you what seems optimal—challenge that expectation? What would change if you truly accepted that every trial is a deliberate choice from a free and wise Creator?
?
If God were obliged to create you in Paradise without trial, you would not be reading this lesson. Since you are here, in a world of tests, what does that reveal about His purpose for you? Are you willing to see your current struggles as part of that divine wisdom, even when you cannot understand it?
?
The poor disbeliever is an example: non-existence would be 'better' for him, yet God creates him. Does your sense of fairness clash with this reality? How can you reconcile God's freedom with His mercy? Where in your life do you need to surrender your own idea of what is 'best' and trust His wisdom instead?

The Debate of al-Ash'ari and al-Jubba'i on the Optimal
Sanusi recounts the famous debate illustrating the impossibility of the optimal theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari refuted the Mu'tazilite doctrine of the optimal using a three-person scenario, and why this leads to a reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
If God must do the optimal, then everyone should die innocent.

This lesson presents a famous dialectical exchange that cut to the core of Mu'tazilite theology. Al-Ash'ari cleverly exposes an internal contradiction in the doctrine that God is obliged to create the best possible world for His servants. By considering the fates of three individuals, he forces al-Jubba'i into an answer that ultimately undermines his own position. Although the passage is incomplete, the argument's force is unmistakable.

Guided Path
1
Read the debate carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation slowly. Notice the back-and-forth structure: al-Ash'ari asks a question, al-Jubba'i answers, then al-Ash'ari challenges the answer.
This is a dialectical style common in kalam.
2
Identify the three persons
The debate centers on three persons: a child who dies before puberty, an adult disbeliever, and an adult believer. Understand their different fates according to al-Jubba'i's Mu'tazilite view.
The child is in Paradise but lower than the adult believer.
3
Follow the logical trap
Al-Ash'ari first gets al-Jubba'i to admit that the child's lower rank is due to lack of work. Then he argues that the child could complain that it would have been optimal to live longer. Al-Jubba'i's reply (that the child would have disbelieved) sets up a devastating counter from the adult disbeliever.
Pay attention to how the argument shifts.
4
Consider the incomplete ending
The passage ends as al-Ash'ari begins to point out that the adult disbeliever (or any disbeliever) could make a similar claim: they would be content with the lowest rank of Paradise. Think about how this would refute the optimal doctrine.
The conclusion is that if God must do the optimal, He would have to make all die as innocent children, which contradicts the existence of adult disbelievers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما أحسنَ مناظرةً وقعَتْ بين الشيخ الأشعري والجبائي في مسألة مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح ! فقالَ الشيخُ للجبائي : ما تقولُ في ثلاثة أشخاص : مات أحدهم قبل البلوغ ، والآخر مات بعد البلوغ كافراً ، والآخرُ ماتَ بعدَهُ مؤمناً ؟ فقال الجبائيُّ : أمَّا الصغيرُ ففي الجنَّةِ (١) ، وأمَّا الكافرُ الكبير ففي النارِ ، وأمَّا الكبيرُ المؤمنُ ففي الدرجاتِ العُلا فقال له الشيخ : ما بال الصغيرِ قُصِرَ بهِ عن درجاتِ الكبير المؤمن ؟ فقالَ الجبائي : لأنَّه لم يعمل قدر عملِهِ فقالَ لهُ الشيخُ : مِنْ حُجَّتِهِ على مذهبكم أن يقول : يا ربِّ ؛ كانَ الأصلح في حقي أن تكون أبقيتني حيّاً حتى أصل بالعمل الدرجة العليا فقال الجبائي : جوابُهُ : أنْ يقولَ اللهُ تعالى له : علمتُ أنَّكُ لو بقيت إلى سنّ التكليف لكفرْتَ ، فتُخلَّدُ في النار ، فالأصلحُ في حقك موتُكَ صغيراً كما فعلْتُ بكَ ؛ لسلامتِكَ بِهِ مِنَ الخلودِ في النار التي هي مِنْ أعظم غنيمةٍ ، فكيف وقد زدتكَ على ذلكَ ما لا يُكَيَّفُ مِنْ نعيمِ الجنَّةِ ؟ ! فقالَ لهُ الشيخُ : فإذاً يقومُ الثالثُ الذي مات كبيراً كافراً بل وكلُّ كافِرٍ مِنْ دَرَكاتِ لظى فيقولُ : يا ربِّ ؛ كنا نرضى منكَ بأدنى مِنْ مرتبةِ
How excellent is the debate that occurred between Shaykh al-Ashʿarī and al-Jubbāʾī on the question of observing well-being and the optimal! So the Shaykh said to al-Jubbāʾī: What do you say about three persons: one died before puberty, another died after puberty as a disbeliever, and another died after it as a believer? Al-Jubbāʾī said: As for the minor, he is in Paradise (1), as for the adult disbeliever, he is in the Fire, and as for the adult believer, he is in the highest ranks. The Shaykh said to him: Why has the minor been lowered from the ranks of the adult believer? Al-Jubbāʾī said: Because he did not do as much work as he did. The Shaykh said to him: He would have an argument according to your school to say: O Lord, the optimal for me would have been that You keep me alive until I reach the highest rank through work. Al-Jubbāʾī said: His answer is that God the Exalted says to him: I knew that if you remained until the age of obligation, you would disbelieve and be eternally in the Fire. So the optimal for you is your death as a minor as I did with you, for your safety from eternal Fire, which is the greatest gain. Indeed, I have increased you beyond that with unquantifiable bliss of Paradise. Then the Shaykh said to him: Then the third one who died as an adult disbeliever, indeed every disbeliever from the depths of Hell would stand and say: O Lord, we would be content from You with the lowest of rank...
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage narrates a famous dialectical debate between Imam al-Ashʿarī and the Muʿtazilite leader al-Jubbāʾī on the doctrine of the optimal (al-aṣlaḥ). The debate is a powerful reductio ad absurdum. Al-Ashʿarī presents a scenario of three persons: a child who dies before puberty, an adult disbeliever, and an adult believer. According to Muʿtazilite principles (based on rational obligation), the child is in Paradise but in a lower rank than the adult believer because he did less work. The child could argue that it would have been optimal for him to have lived longer to gain a higher rank. Al-Jubbāʾī responds that God knew the child would have disbelieved if he lived, so death as a child was optimal to save him from Hell. Al-Ashʿarī then counters that the adult disbeliever would then argue: We would be content with even the lowest rank of Paradise (which is better than Hell), so why were we not kept as minors or created as infants? The argument is that if God is obliged to do the optimal, He would have to make everyone die as innocent children, which contradicts the existence of adult disbelievers. The conclusion (though cut off) is that the Muʿtazilite position leads to absurdity. The passage is incomplete here, but the argumentative force is clear.

Key Terms
مناظرة
الصلاح
الأصلح
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this debate is about predestination versus free will.
Correction: The debate is actually about whether God is obligated to do the optimal for His servants, not about human freedom. The Muʿtazilites held that God must do the best, while Ashʿarites argued God's actions are not subject to rational obligation.

Checkpoint
What scenario does al-Ash'ari present to challenge the Mu'tazilite doctrine of the optimal?
A) Two people, one rich and one poor
B) Three persons: a child, an adult believer, and an adult disbeliever
C) A Muslim and a non-Muslim
D) A prophet and a common believer

Key Takeaway
This debate demonstrates that the Mu'tazilite doctrine of the optimal leads to a logical contradiction: if God must always do the best for each individual, then everyone should die as innocent children, which cannot be reconciled with the existence of adult disbelievers.

Divine Decree
beginner
5 min
Refuting Extreme Predestinarian Claims
Sanusi refutes a deterministic claim on divine action and qadar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that all actions are exclusively from God is rejected in Ash'ari theology and the two false foundations it rests on.

Core Idea
Extending al-tahsin al-‘aqli to qadar leads to theological error.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary confronts an extreme predestinarian claim that denies any human causality. Such a claim, the author argues, is not based on revelation but on a rationalistic method (al-tahsin al-‘aqli) and an illicit attempt to uncover the secret of divine decree. By examining this refutation, we see how Ash'ari theology safeguards divine sovereignty while affirming human accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the structure of the argument sink in.
Notice the author's swift progression from stating the claim to refuting it.
2
Identify the claim being refuted
The passage begins by quoting the claim: 'all actions occur by His will and creation, there is no effect for the servant in any of them!' Pinpoint this and understand what it implies.
This is the extreme view the author opposes.
3
Find the two foundations of the claim
The author says this claim is built on two things: al-tahsin al-‘aqli (rationalistic tashih) and seeking the secret of qadar. Identify these in the text.
These are the bases the author rejects.
4
Understand the scriptural and theological conclusion
The author then cites a hadith forbidding discussion of qadar and concludes with God's absolute sovereignty and the limits of human knowledge. Reflect on how this completes the refutation.
The hadith serves as evidence, and the conclusion affirms orthodox teachings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فالأفعالُ كلُّها واقعة بإرادتِهِ تعالى وخلقِهِ ، لا أثر للعبدِ في شيء منها ! وهذا الكلامُ منهم مبني على التحسين العقلي ، وهو باطل ، وعلى طلب الاطلاع على سرَّ القَدَرِ ، وهو ممَّا نُهينا عن الخوض فيهِ ، قالَ رسولُ اللهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : « إِذَا ذُكِرَ الْقَدَرُ فَأَمْسِكُوا (١) ، والله سبحانَهُ يفعل ما يشاء ، ويحكم ما يريد ، ولا علم لنا بشيءٍ إِلا أَنْ يُعلِّمَنا جلَّ وعزَّ .
And also: all actions occur by His will and creation, there is no effect for the servant in any of them! This speech of theirs is based on rationalistic tashih (al-tahsin al-‘aqli), which is false, and on seeking to know the secret of fate (al-qadar), which we have been forbidden to delve into. The Messenger of God ﷺ said: 'When fate is mentioned, refrain.' And God the Glorified does what He wills and decrees what He desires, and we have no knowledge of anything unless He teaches us, the Exalted and Mighty.
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific claim: that all human actions are exclusively caused by God's will and creation with no human agency. The author identifies this claim as being built on two false foundations: first, rationalistic tashih (al-tahsin al-‘aqli), the idea that reason alone determines good and evil, which is invalid; second, an attempt to penetrate the secret of divine predestination (qadar), which is prohibited. The author invokes a hadith commanding silence when qadar is mentioned, and reaffirms absolute divine sovereignty: God does as He wills, and human knowledge is limited to what He teaches. The passage thus defends Ash'ari orthodoxy against extremist predestinarian views while rejecting rationalist excess.

Key Terms
القَدَر
التَّحْسِين الْعَقْلِي
الإرادة
الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying absolute determinism means denying God's power over all actions.
Correction: This passage refutes the extreme claim that God alone causes all actions with no human role, but it does not deny God's ultimate power; it affirms both divine sovereignty and human accountability, rejecting rationalistic excess.

Checkpoint
What does the author say the claim that 'all actions are by God's will and creation' is based on?
A) Revelation and consensus
B) Rationalistic tashih and investigation of qadar
C) The Sunna and analogy
D) The consensus of the Salaf

Key Takeaway
The claim that all actions are exclusively from God with no human agency is rejected because it relies on rationalistic reasoning and an impermissible inquiry into the secret of fate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to rationalize God's actions using your own sense of justice, falling into the same error as the determinists who relied on al-tahsin al-‘aqli? In what specific situation do you need to submit your reasoning to revelation today?
?
When you face a difficult decree, do you find yourself seeking to understand 'why' God did it, stepping into the forbidden inquiry into the secret of qadar? What would it look like for you to truly 'refrain' when fate is mentioned, and what will you do the next time that urge arises?
?
This passage reminds you that you have no knowledge unless God teaches you. In which area of your life are you relying on your own understanding rather than seeking knowledge from God? What concrete step will you take today to surrender that area to His revelation?
